Parents  and Children  Reflections  on Women  Vows  Air  and Manner  Address  AIR and Manner are often more expressive than words i  That Address in a man for which he is often most valued by a woman is generally owing to his assurance i  A concession should be made with a grace or not at all iii  What a  mere  personal advantage is a plausible Address without morals iii iv  A specious Address frequently abates even a  justly  conceiv'd displeasure vi  There is a Manner in speaking that may be liable to exception when the words without that Manner will bear none vi vii  Anger Displeasure  ANGER and Disgust alter the property at least the appearance of things ii  People hardly ever do any thing in Anger of which they do not repent ii  A person of hard features should not allow himself to be very angry ii  We should not be angry at a person's  not  doing that for us which he has a right either to do or to let alone ii iv ii iv  Faulty people should rather be sorry for the occasion they have given for anger than resent it iii  Nothing can be lovely in a man's eye with which he is displeased iv v  An angry or offended man will not allow to the person with whom he is displeased the merit which is his due iv v  Angry people should never write while their passion holds iv v  Anger unpolishes the most polite v  The Displeasure of friends is to be borne even by an innocent person when it unquestionably proceeds from love v vi  An innocent person may be thankful for that Displeasure in her friend which gives her an opportunity of justifying herself v vi  But then it is ungenerous in a displeased friend not to acknowlege and ask excuse for the mistake which caused the Displeasure the moment he or she is convinced v vi  People of little understanding are most apt to be angry when their sense is called into question vii viii  See  Passion  Apprehensions Fear  THE tender mind drawn in to pursue an irregular adventure will be ready to start at every unexpected appearance i  The most apprehensive beginnings often make the happiest conclusions ii  The certainty even of what we fear is often more tolerable than the suspense ii  The very event of which we are most apprehensive is sometimes that which we ought to wish for ii  Threateners when they have an opportunity to put in force their threats are seldom to be feared ii iii  It is better in a critical and uncertain situation to apprehend without cause than to subject one's self to surprize for want of forethought ii iii  Evils are often greater in  Apprehension  than in  reality  iv  An earnest disavowal of Fear often proceeds from Fear iv v  Few men fear those whom they do not value iv v  B  Beauty Figure  COMELINESS not having so much to lose as Beauty has will hold when Beauty will evaporate or fly off i  Personal advantages are oftener snares than benefits i ii i iii  Tho' Beauty is generally the creature of fancy yet are there some who will be Beauties in every eye i  A good Figure or Person in man or woman gives credit at first sight to the choice of either i  Men moreespecially ought to value themselves rather for their intellectual than personal qualifications i  The pretty fool in all she says in all she does will please we know not why  ibid  Who would grudge the pretty fool her day  ibid  When her butterfly flutters are over she will feel in the general contempt she will meet with the just effects of having neglected to cultivate her better faculties  ibid  While the discreet matron who from youth has maintained her character will find solid veneration take place of airy admiration and more than supply the want of the latter i  A lovely woman whether angry or pleased will appear lovely iii  That cruel distemper which often makes the greatest ravages in the finest faces is not always to be thought an evil v  Goodness and generosity give grace and lustre to beauty vi  Blushes Blushing  SILENCE and Blushes are now no graces  says Lovelrce  with our fine Ladies iii  A distinction is to be made between the confusion which guilt will be attended with and the noble consciousness that overspreads the face of a fine spirit on its being thought capable of an imputed evil iii  Harden'd by frequent public appearances our modern fine Ladies would be as much ashamed as men to be found guilty of blushing  Lovel  iii  The woman who at a gross hint puts her fan before her face seems to be conscious that her Blush is not quite ready  Lovel  iv  See  Modesty  C  Censure Character  THE world illnatur'd as it is said to be is generally more just in giving characters speaking by what it  feels  than is usually imagined i  Those who complain most of the Censoriousness of the world perhaps ought to look  inward  for the occasion oftener than they do i  A wrong step taken by a woman who aims to excel subjects her to more severe censures from the world whose envy she has excited than that world would cast on a less perfect character i  Characters very good or extremely bad are seldom justly given i  We should be particularly careful to keep clear of the faults we censure i ii  Hasty  Censurers subject themselves to the charge of variableness in judgment ii  We should always make allowances for the characters whether bad or good that are given us by interested persons ii  Many of those who have escaped censure have not merited applause ii  Good people  says Lovelace    or rather those who affect to be thought good  are generally so uncharitable that I should not chuse to be good were the consequence to be that I must think hardly of everybody else iii iv iv v  Where reputation is concerned we should not be in haste to censure iii  We should never judge peremptorily on first appearances  ibid  Every man and woman  says Lovelace  is apt to judge of others by what they know of themselves iii iv  A man who proves base to the confidence a woman places in him justifies the harshest censures of such of his enemies as would have persuaded her to reject him iv v  Character runs away with and byasses all mankind v  In the very Courts of Justice Character acquits and condemns as often as fact and sometimes in spite of fact  Lovel ibid  It is not alway just to censure according to events v vi  Difficult situations make seeming occasions for Censure unavoidable vi vii  Censoriousness and narrowness generally prevail with those who affect to be thought more pious than their neighbours vi vii  Very few Ladies would be condemned or even accused in the circle of Ladies were they present vii viii  Human depravity it is feared will oftener justify those who judge harshly than those who judge favourably yet will not good people part with their charity vii viii  Nevertheless it is right to make that charity consist with caution and prudence  ibid  Charity Beneficence Benevolence  BENEVOLENT spirits are sufficiently happy in the noble consciousness that attends their Benevolence i  'Tis a generous pleasure in a Landlord to love to see all his tenants look fat sleek and contented i  That spirit ought not to have the credit of being called bountiful that reserves not to itself the power of being just iii iv  In cases where great good is wished to be done it is grievous to have the will without having the power iii iv  True Generosity is Greatness of soul it incites to do more by a fellowcreature than can be strictly required of us iii iv  Innocent and benevolent spirits are sure to be considered as aliens and to be made to suffer by the genuine sons and daughters of earth v vi  A beneficent person diverted from her course by calamity will resume it the moment she can and go on doing good to all about her as before vi vii  The power of conferring benefits is a godlike power vi vii  A truly generous and beneficent person will in a sudden distress find out the unhappy before the sighing  heart is overwhelmed by it vii  See also  iii viii  See also  iv  A prudent person will suit her Charities to the person's usual way of life vii  See also  iv viii  See also  iv  Persons bless'd with a  will  should be doubly careful to preserve to themselves the  power  of doing good vii viii  The honest industrious labouring poor whom sickness lameness or unforeseen accidents have reduced ought to be the principal objects of our Charity vii viii  Small helps will set forward the sober and industrious poor An ocean of wealth will not be sufficient for the idle and profligate vii viii  It is not Charity to relieve the dissolute if what is given to them deprive the worthy poor of such assistance as would set the wheels of their industry going  ibid  That Charity which provides for the  morals  as well as for the bodily wants of the poor gives a double benefit to the public as it adds to the number of the hopeful what it takes from that of the profligate vii viii  Can there be in the eyes of that God who requires nothing so much from us as acts of beneficence to one another a charity more worthy than that of providing for the souls as well as bodies of our fellowcreatures vii viii  See  Generosity  Church Clergy  THE Church is a good place to begin a reconciliation in if people mean any thing by their prayers  says Lovelace  i  Who that has views either worldly or cruel can go to Church and expect a blessing ii  It is a juster satire upon human nature than upon the Cloth if we suppose that those who have the  best  opportunities of being good are less perfect than others iv v  Professional  as well as  national  reflexions are to be avoided iv v  The Church ought to be the only marketplace for women and domestic excellence their capital recommendation v  A good Clergyman must love and venerate the Gospel he teaches and prefer it to all other learning vi vii  The young Clergyman who throws about to a Christian audience scraps of Latin and Greek from the Pagan Classics shews something wrong either in his heart or head or in both vi vii  A general contempt of the Clergy  even Lovelace confesses  is a certain sign of a man of free principles vi vii  See  Conscience Death Religion  Comedies Tragedies Music Dancing  LIBERTINES love not any Tragedies but those in which they themselves act the parts of tyrants and executioners iii iv  Libertines afraid to trust themselves with serious and solemn reflexions run to Comedies in order to laugh away compunction and to find examples of men as immoral as themselves  ibid  Very few of our Comic Performances give good examples  ibid  Mr  Lovelace  Mr  Sinclair Sally Martin Polly Horton  Miss  Partington  love not Tragedies They have hearts  too feeling  There is enough in the world  say they  to make the heart sad without carrying grief into our diversion and making the distresses of others our own iii iv  The woes of others well represented will unlock and open a tender heart  Lovel  iii iv  The female heart expands and forgets its forms when its attention is carried out of itself at an  agreeable  or affecting Entertainment  Lovel  iii iv  Women therefore should be cautious of the company they go with to public Entertainments  Music and other maidenly amusements are too generally given up by women when married v  Music  says Lovelace  is an amusement that may be necessary to keep a young woman out of more active mischief v  Wine is an opiate in degree How many women  says Lovelace  have been taken at advantage by wine and intoxicating viands v  Dancing is a diversion that women love but they ought to be wary of their company v  Women to women when warm'd by Dancing Music  c  are great darers and provokers v  Persons who sing and play tolerably yet plead inability wish not always to be believed vii viii  Condescension  CONDESCENSION that proceeds from force or even from policy may be often discovered to be forced by observing the eyes and lips ii  Condescension is not meanness iv On the contrary the very word implies dignity iv v  There is a glory in yielding of which a violent spirit can hardly judge iv  By Gentleness and Condescension a requester leaves favourable impressions upon an angry person which on cooler reflection may bring the benefit denied at the time iv  That Condescension which has neither pride nor insult in it gives a grace to the person as well as to the action which demonstrates it iv v  Conscience Consciousness  PERSONS of Conscience will be afraid to begin the world unjustly i  A woman who by surprize or otherwise is brought to swerve loses all that noble selfconfidence which otherwise would have given her a visible superiority over  her tempter ii  See also  ii iii  See also  ii  How uneasy are our reflections upon every doubtful occurrence when we know we have been prevailed upon to do a wrong thing ii iii  It is a satisfaction to a worthy mind to have borne its testimony against the vile actions of a bad one iii  Selfcomplaisancy  is necessary  to carry a woman thro' this life with tolerable satisfaction to herself iv  The look of every person will be construed as a reproach by one who is conscious of having capitally erred vi  See also  iii vi  See also  iii  As to the world and its censures  says Clarissa  however desirous I always was of a fair fame I never thought it right to give more than a second place to the world's opinion vi  See also  i ii iv vi  See also  i ii v  A pure intention void of all undutiful resentments is what must be my consolation  says Clarissa  whatever others may think of the measures I have taken when they come to be known vi vii  Consolation  THOSE who have not deserved illusage have reason to be the easier under it ii  Who would not with patience sustain even a great evil could she persuade herself that it might most probably be dispensed in order to prevent a still  greater  iii  How much lighter on reflection will the same evils sit on the heart of one who has not brought them upon herself than upon one who has iii  See also  ii iii  See also  ii  There is one common point in which all shall meet err widely as they may iii iv  Patience and perseverance overcome the greatest difficulties iii iv  If a person in calamity can consider herself as called  upon to give an example of patience and resignation she will find her mind greatly invigorated iii iv  All nature and every thing in it has its bright and gloomy side We should not always be thinking of the worst iii vi iv vii  My mind  says Clarissa to Lovelace and Tomlinson  is prepared for adversity That I have not deserved the evils I have met with is my Consolation iv v v vi  There must be a world after this to do justice to injured innocence and to punish barbarous perfidy v  We often look back with pleasure on the heaviest griefs when the cause of them is removed v  No one ought to think the worse of herself for having suffered what she could not avoid v  Temporary evils may be borne with because they are  but  temporary v vi  None are made to suffer beyond what they  can  bear and therefore  ought  to bear v vi  We know not the methods of Providence nor what wise ends it may have to serve in its seemingly severe dispensations v vi  A patient and innocent sufferer will look to a world beyond this for its reward v vi  Many happy days may persons greatly unhappy live to see if they will not heighten unavoidable accidents into guilty despondency v vi  We should in an heavy evil comfort  ourselves  as we would in the like circumstances comfort  others  v vi  This world is designed but for a transitory state of probation A good person considering herself as travelling thro' it to a better will put up with all the hardships of the journey in hopes of an ample reward at the end of it v vi  Had I  says Clarissa drawing near her end  escaped the evils I labour under I might have been taken in the midst of some gay promising hope when my heart had  beat high with desire of life and when the vanity of this earth had taken hold of me vi  What happiness on reflexion does that person enjoy who has not acted unworthy of herself in the time of tryal and temptation vi vii  All the  troubles  of this world as well as its joys are but of short duration vi vii  Things the most grievous to human nature at the time often in the event prove the happiest for us vi  See also  vi vii  See also  vii  We remember those we have  long  lost with more pleasure than pain vi vii  Solemn impressions that seem to  weaken  the mind may by proper reflexion be made to  strengthen  it vi vii  Where there is a reliance made on Providence it seldom fails to raise up a new friend for every old one that falls off vi vii  There is often a  necessity  for a considerate person's being unhappy in order to be happy vi vii  Good motions wrought into habit will yield pleasure at a time when nothing else can vi vii  Persons enured to afflictions and who have lived in constant hope of a better life and have no flagrant vices to reproach themselves with are the fittest comforters of friends in distress vii viii  When a man has not great good to comfort himself with it is right  says Lovelace  to make the best of the  little  that may offer vii viii  There never was any discomfort happen'd to mortal man but some little ray of Consolation would dart in if the wretch was not so  much  a wretch as to  draw  instead of  undraw  the curtain to keep it out vii viii  See  Adversity Conscience Death Grief Human  Life  Religion  Controul Authority  NO extraordinary qualifications are to be expected from a man who never as a child was subject to Controul i  Young Ladies on whom parental Controul is known to sit heavily give a man of intrigue room to think that they want to be parents themselves  Lovel  ii iii  A generous mind will then only be jealous of Controul when it imagines its laudable friendships or its generosity are likely to be wounded by it iii  A man by seeming afraid of Controul often subjects himself to it v vi  People awed and controuled tho' but by their own consciousness of inferiority will find fault right or wrong with those of whose rectitude of mind and manners their own culpable hearts give them to be afraid vii viii  See  Duty Parents  and Children  Covetousness Avarice  A COVETOUS man acts as if he thought the world made for himself only i  Covetous people may bear with every one's ill word since they are so solicitous to keep what they prefer to every one's good word i  The difference between obtaining a fame for generosity and incurring the censure of being a miser will not prudently managed cost fifty pounds a year i  A miser's heir may at a small expence obtain the reputation of generosity  ibid  When was an ambitious or covetous mind satisfied with acquisition i  A prodigal man generally does more injustice than a covetous one i  What man or woman who is covetous of wealth or of power desires either for the sake of making a right use of it iv v  Time is the only thing of which we can be allowably covetous since we live but once in this world and when gone are gone from it for ever vii viii  See  Self  Courtship  REVERENCE to a woman in  Courtship  is the less to be dispensed with as generally there is but little of it shewn  afterwards  i  A very ready consent often subjects a woman to contempt i  If a man cannot make a woman in Courtship own herself  pleased  with him it is as  much  and oftentimes  more  to his purpose to make her  angry  with him  Lovel  i  That disgust must be sincere which is conceived on a first visit and confirmed in every after one i  A woman who shews a very great dislike to a  Lover  whom afterward she is induced to  marry  had need to have a double share of prudence to behave unexceptionably to her  husband  i i ii  He who perseveres in his addresses to a woman whose aversion or dislike to him he has no reason to doubt wants the spirit that distinguishes a man i  Very few people in Courtship see each other as they are i ii  Our Courtship days are our best days Favour destroys Courtship distance encreases it  Miss Howe  ii  A woman in Courtship has reason to resent those passions in her Lover which are predominant to that he pretends to have for her ii  One of the greatest indignities that can be cast on a woman in Courtship is for a man to be so profligate as to engage himself in lewd pursuits at the time he pretends his whole heart to be hers ii  A woman accustomed to be treated with obsequiousness will expect obsequiousness to the end of the Courtship chapter  says Miss Howe  iii  The man who expresses high respect to a woman is entitled if not to  acceptance  to  civility  iii  See also  i iv  See also  i  A wise man will not discourage that discretion in a  mistress  which will be his glory and security in a  wife  iv  The woman who in Courtship treats haughtily or ill the man she intends to have gives room for the world to think either That she has a mean opinion of him a high one of herself vi vii  Or That she has not generosity enough to use moderately the power which his great affection gives her vi vii  Such a woman gives reason to free livers to suppose and to presume upon it that the man to whom she intends to give her hand has no share in her heart vi vii  And if she shew that regard to him  after  marriage of which she shewed none  before  it will be construed as a compliment to the  Husband  made at the expence of the  Wife  's and even of the  Sex  's delicacy vi vii  Such a one will teach the world by  her  example to  despise  the man whom when her Husband she would wish it to  respect ibid  To condescend with dignity to command with kindness and sweetness of manners are points to be aimed at by a wise woman in Courtship vi vii  She should let her Lover see that she has generosity to approve of and reward a wellmeant service  That she has a mind that lifts her above the little captious follies which some attribute to the sex  That she resents not if ever she has reason to be displeased with pride or thro' petulance  That by insisting on little points she aims not to come at or to secure great ones perhaps not proper to be carried  Nor leaves room to suppose that she thinks she has so much cause to doubt her own merit as to make it needful to put her Lover upon disagreeable or arrogant trials  But lets reason be the principal guide of her actions  And then she will hardly ever fail of that respect which will make her judgment  after  marriage consulted sometimes with a preference to a man's own at other times as a delightful confirmation of his vi vii  When  judgment  is at a loss to determine the choice of a Lady who has several Lovers  fancy  may the more allowably predominate vi vii  Women cannot put the question to a Lover Whether he mean honourably or not in his address without affronting their own virtue and personal graces vi vii  They should therefore never admit of the address of a Libertine  The woman who in Courtship uses ill the man she intends to have reflects not on the obligations her pride is laying her under to him for his  patience  with her vi vii  See  Advice  to Women  Husband  and Wife  Libertine Love Marriage Parents  and are never asunder ii iii  Credulity permits us not till we suffer by it to see the defects of those of whom we think highly iii iv  We are all very ready to believe what we like iv  See  Courtship Love Lover  Cruelty Hardheartedness  THAT Cruelty which children are permitted to shew to birds and other animals will most probably exert itself on their fellowcreatures when at years of maturity iii iv  Let the parents of such a child expect a Lovelace iv v iv vi  When we reflect upon the Cruelties daily practised upon such of the animal creation as are given us for food or which we ensnare for our diversion we shall be obliged to own  says even Lovelace  that there is more of the savage in human nature than we are aware of iii iv  Infinite beauties are there to be found in a weeping eye  Lovel  iii iv  Hardheartedness is an essential in the character of a Libertine iii iv iv  No heart bursts  says the savage Lovelace  be the occasion for sorrow what it will which has the kindly relief of tears iv v  See  Libertine Tears  D  Death Dying  MELANCHOLY objects and subjects will at times impress the most profligate spirits  They should not therefore be run away from  iv  What is Death but a cessation from mortal life vi  It is but the finishing of an appointed course  ibid  The refreshing Inn after a fatiguing journey  ibid  The end of a life of cares and troubles  ibid  Those men who give themselves airs of bravery on reflecting upon the last scenes of others may be expected if sensible at the time to behave the most pitifully in their own vi vii  What a dreadful thing is Death to a person who has not one comfortable reflexion to revolve vi vii  What would I give  says the departing Belton  to have but one year of life before me and to have the same sense of things I now have  ibid  See also the dying Belton's pleas to his Physician and treatment of him and of his own Sister because they could give him no hope  vi  to  vii  The seeds of Death are sown in us when we begin to  live and grow up till like rampant weeds they choak the tender flower of life vi vii  In beholding the Death of a friend we are affected as well by what must one day be our own case as by his agonies vi vii  To be cut off by the sword of injured friendship is the most dreadful of all Deaths next to Suicide vi vii  Resignation in Death and reliance on the Divine mercies give great comfort to the friends of the dying vi vii vii viii  A good conscience only can support a person in a sensible and gradual Death vi vii vii  It is a choice comfort at the winding up of our short story  says Clarissa  to be able to say  "I have rather  suffered  injuries than  offered  them"  vii  Nothing that is of consequence should be left to be done in the last incapacitating hours of life vii  See Clarissa's noble behaviour in the agonies of Death  vii  to  viii  All sentiments of worldly grandeur vanish at that unavoidable moment which decides the destiny of men vii viii  What in the last solemn moments must be the reflexion of those if capable of reflection whose study and pride it has been to seduce the innocent and to ruin the weak the unguarded and the friendless perhaps too by themselves made friendless vii viii  See the shocking and outragious behaviour of Sinclair at her Death  vii viii  seq    See also the violent Death of Lovelace  vii  seq  viii  seq    What are twenty or thirty years to look back upon vii viii  In a long life what friends may we not have to mourn for  ibid  What temptations may we not have to encounter with  ibid  In the loss of a dear friend it is an high satisfaction  to be able to reflect that we have no acts of unkindness to reproach ourselves with vii viii  Time only can combat with advantage very heavy deprivations vii viii  Nature will be given way to till sorrow has in a manner exhausted itself then reason and religion will come in seasonably with their powerful aids to raise the drooping heart  ibid  See  Consolation Grief Religion  Delicacy Decency Decorum  MUCH disagreeable evil will arise to a woman of the least Delicacy from a Husband who is given to wine i  What young woman of Delicacy would be thought to have  inclinations  so violent that she could not conquer them or a  will  so stubborn that she would not at the entreaty and advice of her friends attempt the conquest i ii  Punctilio is out of doors the moment a Daughter clandestinely quits her Father's house ii  How inexcuseable are those giddy creatures who in the same hour leap from a Parent's window to a Husband's bed  ibid  Numberless are the reasons that might be given why a woman of the least Delicacy should never think of going off with a man ii iii  A woman who goes off with a man has no room either to practise Delicacy herself or to expect it from the man iii iv  A consent in some nice Lovecases were better taken for granted than asked for iii iv  Few very few men are there who have Delicacy enough to enter into those parts of the female character which are its glory and distinction iii iv  Overniceness may be underniceness iv v  Men  need not  give indelicate hints to women on subjects that relate to themselves  Lovel  iv v  A man who is gross in a woman's company  adds he  ought to be knock'd down with a club  ibid  Delicate women make delicate women and also detent men v  There are points so delicate that it is a degree of dishonour to have a vindication of one's self from them thought necessary v vi  The free things that among us Rakes  says Belford  pass for wit and spirit must be shocking stuff to the ears of persons of Delicacy v vi  See  Advice  to Women  Courtship Duty Libertine Love Marriage Men  and Women c  Despondency Despair  IF we despond there can be no hope of cure iii vi iii vi  To despond is to add sin to sin iv  When a profligate man on being overtaken by a dangerous sickness or inevitable calamity desponds what consolation can be given him either from his past life or his future prospects vi  This is the cause of my despair  says Belton  that God's  justice  cannot let his  mercy  operate for my comfort vi vii  See  Consolation  Deviation  To condemn a Deviation and to follow it by as great a one what is it but to propagate a general corruption ii  The Deviation of a person of eminence is more inexcusable than that of a common person iii  In unhappy situations it will be difficult even for worthy persons to avoid sometimes departing from the simple truth iii  How necessary is it then for such persons to be careful that they do not by their own inconsideration involve themselves in difficulties  Worthy persons if inadvertently drawn into a Deviation will endeavour instantly to recover their lost ground that they may not bring error into habit iii  A criminal Deviation in one friend is likely to cast a shade upon the other iii  To the pure every little Deviation  says Lovelace  seems offensive iii iv  One devious step at first setting out frequently leads a person into a wilderness of doubt and error iii iv  When we are betrayed into a capital Deviation lesser Deviations will hardly be avoidable iv v  She who is too ready to excuse a wilful Deviation in another renders her own virtue suspectable  Jam Harl  vii  See  Guilt Human  Nature  Dignity Quality  UPON true Quality and hereditary Distinction if sense be not wanting honours and affluence sit easy i  If we assume a Dignity and disgrace not by arrogance our assumption everybody will treat us with respect and deference i ii  Hereditary Dignity conveys more disgrace than honour to descendants who have not merit to adorn it ii  Gentleman  is a title of distinction which a prince may not deserve ii iii  The first Dignity ought to be accompanied with the first merit iii iv  Grandeur  says Lovelace  always makes a man's face shine in a woman's eye v  People who are fenced in either by their Years or Quality should not  says Lovelace  take freedoms that a man of spirit ought to resent from others v vi  True Dignity admits not of pride or arrogance v vi  Some men have a native Dignity in their manner which will procure them more regard by a look than others can obtain by the most imperious commands vi vii  The man who is good by choice as well as by education has that  Quality  in himself  that true Dignity  which ennobles human nature and without which the most dignified by birth or rank are ignoble vi vii  Women who will not assume some little Dignity and exact respect from men will render themselves cheap and perhaps have their modesty and diffidence repaid with scorn and insult  Miss Howe  vii viii  See  Advice  to Women  Courtship Delicacy Libertine  c  Double  Entendre  IT is an odious thing in a man to look sly and leering at a woman whose modesty is invaded by another by indecent hints or Double Entendre iii  What a grossness is there in the mind of that man who thinks to reach a Lady's heart by wounding her ears iii  Wellbred men who think themselves in virtuous company will not allow in themselves such liberties of speech as tho' not free enough for open censure are capable of conveying impure images to the heart iii  Men who go out of their way to hint free things must either be guilty of absurdity meaning nothing or meaning something of rudeness  ibid  Obscenity is so shameful even to the guilty that they cannot hint at it but under a double meaning iv  Even Lovelace declares that he never did nor ever will talk to a Lady in a way that modesty will not permit her to answer him in vii viii  See  Delicacy  Dress Fashions Elegance  THE genius of a man who is fond of his Person or Dress seldom strikes deep into intellectual subjects i  A man vain of his person endeavouring to adorn it frequently renders himself ridiculous  ibid  Women owe to themselves and to their Sex to be always neat and never to be surprised by accidental visitors in such a dishabille as would pain them to be seen in ii  All that hoops are good for  says Miss Howe  is to clean dirty shoes and to keep fellows at distance ii  The mind is often indicated by outward Dress iii  Homely persons the more they endeavour to adorn themselves the more they expose the defects they want to hide iii iv  If women  says Lovelace  would made themselves appear as elegant to an Husband as they were desirous to appear to him while a Lover the  Rake  which all women love would last longer in the  Husband  than it generally does iii iv  A woman who would preserve a Lover's respect to her person will be careful of her appearance before him when in dishabille iii iv  Full Dress creates dignity augments consciousness and keeps at distance an encroacher iii iv  An elegant woman in her earliest hour will for  her own  pleasure be as nice as others in full dress iii iv  Elegant Dress contributes greatly to keep passion alive v  Dress gives great advantage to women who have naturally a genteel air and have been well educated v  Persons who thro' misfortunes chuse not to dress should not however give up neatness v vi  A Fop takes great pains to hang out a  sign  by his Dress of what he has in his  shop  vi  A clumsy Beau seems to owe himself a double spite making his ungracefulness appear the more ungraceful by his tawdriness in Dress  Lovel  vi  Singularity in Dress shews something wrong in the mind  ibid  Plain Dress for an ordinary man or woman implies at least modesty and procures kind quarter even from the censorious vi vii vi viii  The Fashion or Dress that becomes one person frequently misbecomes another vi vii  Nature and Ease should be the guides in Dress or Fashion vi vii  See  Advice  to Women  Delicacy Dignity  Duelling  A MAN of honour cannot go to law for verbal abuses given by people entitled to wear swords  L  i  Duelling is so fashionable a part of brutal bravery that a good man is often at a loss so to behave as to avoid incurring either mortal guilt or general contempt i ii  Those who throw contempt on a good man for chusing rather to pass by a verbal injury than imbrue his hands in blood know not the measure of true magnanimity  ibid  'Tis much more noble to forgive and much more manly to despise than to resent an injury  ibid  A man of spirit should too much disdain the man who is capable of doing him wilfully a mean wrong to put his life upon equal value with his own  ibid  What an absurdity is it in a man to put it in the power of one who has done him a  small  injury to do him as it may happen and those who love him an irreparable one  ibid  What a flagrant partiality is it in those men who can  themselves  be guilty of crimes which they justly hold unpardonable in their nearest female relations vi vii  Yet cannot commit them without doing such injuries to other families as they think themselves obliged to resent unto death when offered to their own  ibid  An innocent man ought not to run an equal risk with a guilty one vii viii  He who will arrogate to himself the province of the Almighty who has declared  that vengeance is His  ought to tremble at what may be the consequence vii viii  May it not in case of the  offended  person's giving the challenge be suitable to the Divine justice to punish the  presumptuous innocent  by the hand of the  self defending guilty  reserving him for a future day of vengeance vii viii  Life is a short stage when longest If Heaven will afford a wicked man time for repentance who shall dare to deny it him vii viii  The conscience of the offender when it shall please God to strike it shall be sharper than an avenger's sword vii viii  Duelling is not only an usurpation of the Divine prerogative but it is an insult upon magistracy and good government vii viii  'Tis an impious act 'tis an attempt to take away a life that ought not to depend upon a private sword  ibid  An act the consequence of which is to hurry a soul all its sins upon its head into perdition endangering also that of the poor triumpher since neither intend to give to the other that opportunity for repentance which each presumes to hope for himself vii viii  Where shall the evil of Duelling stop Who shall avenge on the avenger vii viii  Who would not wish that the aggressor should be still the  guilty  aggressor  ibid  Often has the  more  guilty been the vanquisher of the  less  guilty  ibid  See  Guilt Libertine  Duty Obedience  A GOOD child will not seek to exculpate herself at the expence of the most revered characters i  If we suffer by an act of Duty or even of generosity we have this comfort on reflexion that the fault is in others not in ourselves i  Altho' our parents or friends should not do everything for us that we may wish or expect it becomes us nevertheless to be thankful to them for the benefits they have actually conferred on us i  A good child upon ill terms with her parents tho' hopeless of success should leave no means unattempted to reconcile herself to them were it but to acquit herself to herself i  A sufferer may not be able to forbear complaining of the ill treatment she meets with from her parents but it may go against her to have even the person to whom she complains take the same liberties with them i  The want of reward is no warrant for us to dispense with our Duty  ibid  The merit of Obedience consists in giving up an inclination i  In reciprocal Duties the failure on one side justifies not a failure on the other i vi i ii vii  Prudence and Duty will enable a person to overcome the greatest difficulties i  Where is the praiseworthiness of Obedience if it be only paid in instances where we give up nothing i ii  If a passion  can  be conquered it is a sacrifice a good child owes to indulgent parents especially if they would be unhappy if she made not such a sacrifice i  See also  i ii  See also  i  No independency of fortune can free a child from her filial Duty ii  Nor ought any change of circumstances to alter her notions of Duty iii  A Duty exacted with too much rigour is often attended with fatal consequences iii  The Duty of a child to her parents may be said to be anterior to her very birth iii  What is the precise stature or age at which a good child may conclude herself absolved from her filial Duty iii  A good person cannot look with indifference on any part of a vow'd Duty iii  A worthy person will make it her prayer as well as her endeavour that whatever trials she may be called upon to undergo she may not behave unworthily in them and may come out amended by them iii  A daughter who chearfully gives up an inclination to  the judgment of her parents may be said to have laid them under obligation to her iii iv  Can a fugitive Daughter enjoy herself while her Parents are in tears iv  Other people's not performing their Duty is no excuse for the neglect of ours  says even Lovelace  v vi  The world is too apt to set itself in opposition to a general Duty vi vii  General Duties ought not to be weakened by our endeavouring to justify a single person if faulty however unhappily circumstanced  ibid  There is no merit in performing a Duty vi vii  A dutiful Daughter gives an earnest of making a dutiful and obliging Wife vi vii  Duty upon principle will oblige to an uniformity of Duty in every relation of life  ibid  Rigour makes it difficult for sliding virtue to recover itself vii viii  See  Parents  and Children  E  Education  ENCOURAGEMENT and approbation bring to light talents that otherwise would never have appeared ii  There is a docible season a learningtime in youth which suffered to elapse and no foundation laid seldom returns iii iv  Some genius's like some fruits ripen not till late  ibid  Industry and perseverance in study will do prodigious things  ibid  What an uphill labour must it be to a learner who has those first rudiments to master at twenty years of age which others are taught at ten  ibid  Parents ought to cultivate the minds of their Daughters and inspire them with early notions of reserve and distance to men  Lovel  iv  It is not enough that a youth be put upon doing acts of beneficence he must be taught to do them from proper motives iv  A pious end and a crown of glory are generally the natural fruits of a virtuous Education vii viii  The person who aims at acquiring too many things will hardly excel in any vii viii  Improvement must attend upon those who are more ready to hear than to speak vii viii prudence and distinguish'd talents seem to be sprinkled thro' the world to do credit by their example to religion and virtue i  No one should plead the errors of another in justification of his own i ii ii  Persons who are fond of being thought of as examples should look into themselves watch and  fear  iv  Dearly do I love  says Lovelace speaking of Miss Rawlins  to engage with the Preceptgivers and Examplesetters v  The Example at church of persons conspicuous for virtue rank and sense gives a high credit to religion vi vii  See  Religion Virtue  Expectation  THERE is more joy in expectation and preparation than in fruition  be the pursuit what it will  i  Mankind cheat themselves by their raised Expectations of pleasure in prospect i ii  Very seldom is it that high Expectations are so much as tolerably answered iii iv  The joys of Expectation are the highest of all our joys v  Eyes  A WEEPING Eye indicates a gentle heart iii iv  Sparkling Eyes  says Lovelace  when the poetical tribe have said what they will of them are an infallible sign of a rogue or room for a rogue in the heart iii iv  The Eye is the casement at which the heart generally looks out  Lovel  vi  Many a woman who will not shew herself at the door has tipt the sly the intelligible wink from the window  Lovel ibid  See  Tears  F  Faults Folly Failings Error  A MAN who gives the world cause to have an ill opinion of him ought to take the consequence of his own Faults i  Who ever was in Fault Self being judge i  What a hero or heroine must that person be who can conquer a constitutional Fault i  See also  i i ii  See also  i  It is not enough for a person convicted of a Fault to own it if he amend it not i  An enemy wishes not a man to be without the Faults he upbraids him with i  A woman who gives better advice than she takes doubles the weight of her own Faults i ii  Faults which arise from generous attachments are not easily detected iii  No man has a right to be displeased at freedoms taken with him for Faults which he is not ashamed to confess iii iv  It ought to be our care that whatever Errors we fall into they should be those of our judgment and not of our will iii iv  Great Faults and great Virtues are often found in the same persons iv  Repetition of Faults revives the remembrance of Faults forgiven iv  When we are drawn into an Error we should take care to make as few people as possible suffer by the consequence of it iv v  One Crime is generally the parent of another v  It is kind to endeavour to extenuate the Fault of one who is more ready to reproach than to excuse herself v vi  People are apt to make allowances for such Faults in others as they will not mend in themselves vii viii  Wicked men will often abuse people for the consequence of their own Faults v vi  Worthy minds should not be more ready to fly from the Rebuke than from the Fault vi  We may be mortified by a calamity brought upon ourselves but this too often rather for the calamity's than the Fault's sake vi vii  Persons who will not be at the pains of correcting constitutional Faults or Failings frequently seek to gloss them over by some nominal virtues vii viii  See  Guilt  Favour  FAVOURS are ask'd by some with an air that calls for rejection i  To exalt the person we favour above his merit is but to depreciate him i  See also  i ii  See also  i  A worthy mind will not ask a Favour till it has considered whether it is fit to be granted ii  In our expectations of Favours we should divest ourselves of  self  so far as to leave to others the option they have a right to make ii vi ii vi  Awe reverence and apprehended prohibition make a Favour precious  Lovel  ii iii  To request a Favour is one thing to challenge it as our due is another iv  A petitioner has no right to be angry at a repulse if he has not a right to demand what he sues for as a debt iv  The grace with which a Favour is conferred may be as acceptable as the Favour itself vii viii  Flattery Compliments  IF we have power to oblige our Flatterers will tell us any thing sooner than what they know we dislike to hear i  Complimental flourishes are the poison of female minds i  Hyperbolic Compliments are elevated absurdities ii  A man who flatters a woman hopes either to find her a fool or to make her one  ibid  It is not always wrong to take the man at his word who pretending to depreciate himself lays out for a compliment ii iii iii iv  Undue compliments ought to be looked upon as affronts to the understanding of the person to whom they are addressed iii  Women by encouraging Flatterers teach men to be hypocrites yet at other times stigmatize them for deceivers  Lovel  iii iv  Great men do evil and leave it to their Flatterers to find a reason for it afterwards vi vii  Officious persons are always at hand to flatter or sooth the passions of the affluent vi vii  Many persons endeavouring to avoid the imputation of Flattery or Hypocrisy run into rusticity or illmanners vii viii  See  Advice  to Women  Fond Fondness  THE woman must expect to bear slights from the  husband  of whom she was too visibly fond as a  lover  i  Fondness spoils more wives than it makes grateful ones  Solmes  i  The fond mother ever makes a harden'd child i  Copy maids make fond wives  says Mr Solmes  i ii  The Fondness of a wife to an husband whom in courtship she despised for mental imperfections must be imputed either to dissimulation or to very indelicate motives i ii  We are apt to be fond of any body that will side with us when we are oppressed or provoked ii  Fondness and Toying between a married pair before company  Lovelace  himself condemns not only as indiscreet but as indecent and scandalous iv  Single Ladies who shew too visible a Fondness for a man discharge him fom all complaisance iv v  Single Ladies should never be witnesses to those freedoms between fond husbands and wives tho' ever so much the wives friends which they would not have offered to themselves  Lovel  v vi  Forgiveness Pardon  MANY a young offender against modesty and decency has been confirmed a libertine by a too easy Forgiveness iii  See also  i iv  See also  i  An easy Forgiveness where a person  ought  to be forgiven will increase the obligation with a mind not ungenerous iv v  A negative Forgiveness is an ungracious one iv v  The person who would exact a promise of Pardon tacitly acknowleges that he deserves it not iv v  May those be forgiven  prays Clarissa in the height of her calamities  who hinder my father from forgiving me and this shall be the harshest thing relating to them that falls from my pen v vi  An accidental and unpremeditated Error carries with it the strongest plea for Forgiveness v vi  Tell Mr  Lovelace nobly says Clarissa  that I am endeavouring to bring my mind to such a frame as to be able to pity him and that I shall not think myself qualified for the state I am aspiring to if after a few struggles more I cannot  forgive  him too vi vii vi vii  Nothing can be more wounding than a generous Forgiveness vi  The easy Pardon perverse children meet with when they have done the most rash and undutiful thing they can do occasions many to follow their example vi vii  To be forgiven by injured Innocents is necessary  Lovelace thinks  to the divine Pardon vi vii  Men are less unforgiving than women  Lovel  vi vii  Friendship  FRIENDSHIP should never give a byas against judgment i  True Friendship admits not of reserve  ibid  How shall we expect to avoid the censure of our enemies if our Friends will not hold a lookingglass before us to let us see our imperfections in it i  Friend should judge Friend as an indifferent person would be supposed to judge of him i  It is natural for the person who has the misfortune of losing old Friends to be desirous of making new ones i  Such a difference in temper and constitution in two young Ladies as excludes all imaginary rivalship may be the cement of a firm Friendship between them i  The part of a true Friend is to sooth or conciliate rather than to stimulate or provoke the anguish of a complaining spirit ill at ease with her nearest relations i i  A brother may not be a Friend but a Friend will always be a brother i ii  An ingenuous and worthy mind will say with  Clarissa  "Spare me not  because  I am your Friend but rather for that very reason spare me not"  ii  No true Friend can ask to be relieved from a distress which would involve a Friend in as deep a one ii  But if with a small inconvenience to ourselves we could relieve our Friend from a great one I would not  says Miss Howe  admit the refuser into the outermost fold of my heart ii  See also  ii ii  See also  ii  To be displeased with a Friend for telling us our faults is putting ourselves into the inconvenient situation of royalty and out of the way of amendment ii  Veneration is hardly compatible with that sweet familiarity which is necessary to unite two persons in the bands of Friendship  ibid  The person who has been misled is obliged as well in prudence as in generosity and justice that her own error may not spread to caution a trulybeloved Friend not to fall into the like iii  Freely to give reproof and thankfully to receive it is an indispensable condition of true Friendship iii  An apology made for an honest and friendly freedom is a sort of civil affront iii  It is kind  tho' it may be difficult  to conceal from a dear Friend those griefs which cannot be relieved iii iv  Misfortunes give a call to discharge the noblest offices of Friendship  ibid  Great minds carry their Friendship beyond accidents and ties of blood iii iv  Fervent Friendships seldom subsist between two sisterbeauties both toasts iii iv  There is a consentaneousness in some minds which will unite them stronger to each other in a few hours than can be done in years with some others whom yet we see not with disgust iv  An active spirit in one Friend and a passive one in the other is likely to make their Friendship durable v  A great error ought less to be excused in one we value than in one to whom we are indifferent v vi  True Friendship will make a person careful to shun every appearance that may tend to debase it by selfish or sordid views v vi  No Friendship but what is virtuous can be worthy of that sacred name v vi  There are Friendships that are only bottledeep vi vi vii  Friendships with gay people who became intimate because they were gay the reason for their first intimacy ceasing will fade vi  The Friendship of gay people and of free livers ought more properly to be called  Companionship ibid  Ladies conspicuously worthy give significance to those whom they honour with their intimacy vi  The ties of pure Friendship are more binding and tender than those of Nature vi  It is disgraceful to be thought to be the intimate Friend of a profligate and incorrigible man vi  There is an exalted pleasure in intellectual Friendship that cannot be tasted in the gross fumes of sensuality vi  Warmth becomes Friendship when our Friend is struggling with undeserved calamity vi  I have no notion  says Miss Howe  of coolness in Friendship be it disguised or distinguished by the name of  Prudence  or what it will  ibid  It is not every one who has a soul capable of Friendship vi  One day profligate men will be convinced that what they call Friendship is chaff and stubble and that nothing is worthy of that sacred name that has not virtue for its base vi vii  The good opinion we have entertain'd of a person  we have once thought worthy of it is not to be lightly given up vi vii  Friendship generally speaking is too fervent a flame for female minds to manage  Col Morden  vii viii  A light that but in few of their hands burns steady and often hurries the Sex into flight and absurdity and like other extremes is hardly ever durable  Col Morden ibid  Marriage which is the highest state of Friendship generally absorbs the most vehement Friendship of female to female  ibid  What female mind is capable of two fervent Friendships at the same time  ibid  The following are the requisites  according to Col Morden  of fervent and durable female Friendship to wit That both should  like Clarissa and Miss Howe  have enlarged hearts a good education and minds thirsting after virtuous knowlege  That they should be nearly of equal fortunes in order to be above that dependence on each other which frequently destroys the familiarity that is the cement of Friendship  That each should excel in different ways that there might not be room for either to envy the other  That each should see something in the other to fear as well as to love  That it should be an indispensable condition of their Friendship each to tell the other of her failings and to be thankful for the freedom taken  That the one should be  by Nature  gentle the other  made so  by her love and admiration of her Friend vii viii  G  Gaming  GAMING is equally a waster of time and talents i  It is making my friends a very ill compliment  says Clarissa  to suppose they wish to be possessed of what belongs to me and I should be very unworthy if I desir'd  to make myself a title to what is theirs vii viii  Except for trifles what prudent person would submit to Chance what they are already sure of  ibid  High Gaming is an immorality a sordid vice the child of avarice and a direct breach of that commandment which forbids us to covet what is our neighbour's  ibid  Generosity Generous Minds  RESERVES are painful to open and free spirits i  Generous minds are rather to be invited than intimidated i  A generousspirited woman to be happy should take care not to marry a sordid man i  A generous mind will love the person who corrects her in love the better for the correction i  The tenderest and most generous minds when harshly treated frequently become the most inflexible i ii  Generosity engages the noblyminded as strongly as Love ii  Undue displeasure when appearing to a generous mind undue will procure to the supposed offender high amends ii  Nobleminded persons in the exertion of their munificence silently reproach the rest of the world ii  Tho' a generous person may wish she had not been laid under obligations for a benefit unrequestedly conferr'd on herself or her dependents yet she cannot but love the obliger the more for the exertion of a spirit so like her own ii  A generous person highly praised will endeavour to deserve the good opinion of the applauder that she may not at once disgrace his judgment and her own heart ii  A truly generous and candid mind will often make excuses for other people in cases where it would not have allow'd of one for itself ii  See also  ii ii  See also    A generous mind cannot abuse a generous confidence ii iii  A truly generous spirit will in requisite cases give advice against itself iii  A frank or openminded person at once where he likes mingles minds and is forward to dissipate diffidences iii  A generous spirit cannot enjoy its happiness without communication iii  The person who has the advantage in an argument and is incapable of insult or triumph upon it will disappoint envy and subdue illwill iii iv  True Generosity is more than Politeness it is more than good Faith it is more than Honour it is more than Justice since all these are but duties iii iv  The man who would be thought generous must first be just iii iv  A generous mind will not take pleasure in vexing even those by whom it has been distress'd iv v  Leave should not be waited for to do a right a just a generous thing if it be in one's power to do it v  It may be very generous in one person to offer what it would be ungenerous in another to accept v vi  A Person of a mind not ungenerous will rather be sorry for having given an offence than displeased at being amicably told of it vi  Generous minds are always of kin  ibid  A generous mind must be uneasy when it is laid under obligations which are beyond its power to return vi  Love and Gratitude will not be narrow'd down to meer familyconsiderations vii viii  It is generous to take the part of an absent person if not flagrantly culpable vii viii  Generosity is the happy medium between Parsimony and Profuseness vii viii  A generous mind will not scruple to give advantage  to a person of merit tho' not always to her own advantage vii viii  See  Friendship Goodness  Goodness Grace  A GOOD Person will not wilfully incur the censure even of an adversary i  A good man need not be afraid that his conduct should be pry'd into i  Goodness is Greatness i  A good person far from being guilty of a falshood will not have recourse to equivocation i  People  says Lovelace  who act like angels ought to have angels to deal with ii  How great a satisfaction is it to a good mind to be able to reflect that it has rather  suffered  than  offered  wrong ii iii  A good man will not make the slumbers of a worthy woman uneasy iii  A worthy person will be always ready to draw favourable conclusions on the actions and words of others iv v  A good person will wish to make every one happy even to her very servants iv v  Goodness and generosity of sentiments give grace and lustre to beauty vi  A good woman will have other views in living than the common ones of eating sleeping dressing visiting  c  vi  Goodness must be uniform vii  The word Grace is the Rake's Shibboleth There are no hopes of one who can make a jest either of it or of him who uses it vii viii  A goodnatured and polite person will not expose even pretenders to Science in their absence to the ridicule of lively spirits vii viii  See  Friendship Virtue  Gratitude Ingratitude  IT is Ingratitude and Tyranny in a woman to use a man the worse for his respect to her i  A thankful spirit is the same as a joyful one iv  We must be greatly sensible of the Ingratitude of those we love vi  To take advantage of an innocent creature's good opinion to her own detriment or ruin is the most ungrateful wickedness that can be committed by man vi vii  Particular instances of Ingratitude in another to us should not be permitted to narrow and contract our charity into general doubt or jealousy vii viii  Grief Sorrow Grievances  WHEN grievances are to be enumerated slight matters are often thrown in to make weight that otherwise would not have been complained of i  That silence wants not either merit or amiableness which is owing to the person's being afraid of discovering by his  voice  the depth of his concern ii  What a poor passive machine is the body when the mind is disorder'd ii  Sorrow makes an ugly face odious  Lovel  iv vi v vii  Those who mourn for a lost friend will find their Grief very much abated when they are themselves attack'd by a dangerous or painful illness  Lovel  iv  Grief  says Lovelace  is a slow worker and gives time to pop in a little joy between its sullen fits iv  It is the humble silent Grief that only deserves pity iv v  How anxiously do we pray for the life of a dear child in its illness which when grown to maturity we have reason to wish had not been granted to our prayers vi vii  Those who fly from home to avoid an heavy scene labour under more distress in the intermediate suspense than they could have done were they to be present at it vii  Seasonable and necessary employments should be found  out to amuse and to divert persons suffering under violent Grief or loss of dearest friends vii viii  It is natural for us in every deep and sincere Grief to interest in it all we know vii viii  Grief  for the loss of friends  may be mellow'd by time into remembrances more sweet than painful vii viii  See  Adversity Consolation  Guilt Vice Wickedness Evil  Habits  Evil  Courses  HABITS are not easily changed i vii i viii  Vice is a coward and will hide its head when steadily opposed by an advocate for virtue i ii  What must be the force of evil Habits in a man who  thinks  right yet disgrace his knowlege by  acting wrong  iii  The guilty eye will sink under an examining one that is innocent iii  The Guilty less bear the detecting truth than the innocent do the degrading falshood iv  Bad men take more pains to be wicked than it would cost them to be good iv v iv v  The sun shines alike upon the bad and the good but the guilty mind it cannot illuminate v  Every vice generally brings on it own punishment v  The injured will often sweetly sleep when the injurer cannot close his eyes  ibid  There can hardly be a greater punishment hereafter  says Lovelace reflecting on his last outrage on Clarissa  than that which I at this instant experience in my own remorse  ibid  What a detection must ever fall to the lot of  Guilt says Lavelace on Clarissa's behaviour in the Penknife scene  were it given to  Innocence  always thus nobly to exert itself v vi  Many people are deterr'd from Evil rather by the fear of detection than by principle v vi  To plunder a wreck and to rob at a fire are the most barbarous of all villainies vi  Sins presumptuously committed against knowlege and against warning are the most unpardonable of all others vi vii  Those who cannot stand the shock of public shame ought to be doubly careful that they incur not private Guilt which may bring them to it vi vii  Guilt when detected is literally speaking its own punisher even in this world since it makes the haughtiest spirits look like miscreants vi vii  Evil Courses can no longer yield pleasure than while thought and reflection can be kept off vii viii  See  Innocence Ingratitude Libertine Remorse Repentance  H  Happiness Content  IT is happy for a person to leave the world possessed of every one's love i  Happiness and Riches are two things and very seldom meet together i  Were we perfect which no one  can  be we could not be happy in this life even in the usual acceptation of the word unless those with whom we have to deal and more especially those who have any controul over us were governed by the principles by which we ourselves are directed i  To  know  we are happy and not to leave it to afterreflection to look back upon the preferable Past with a heavy and selfaccusing heart is the highest of human felicities ii  What a happiness must that man know who moves regularly to some laudable end and has nothing to reproach himself with in his progress to it iv v  The heiress to Content is the richest heiress that can be sought after v vi  See  Friendship Generosity Goodness  Health  SOUND Health will make the soul and body pleased with each other i  Poverty is the mother of Health ii Temperance will give Health and Vigour to an originally tender constitution ii iii  Health disposes us to be pleased with ourselves and then we are in a way to be pleased with every one else vi  In Health every hope rises upon us every hour presents itself to us on dancing feet  ibid  What Mr Addison says of Liberty may with still greater propriety be said of Health for what is Liberty itself without Health  It makes the gloomy face of Nature gay  Gives beauty to the Sun and pleasure to the Day  ibid  Men of very strong bodily Health seldom know how to pity the sick or infirm vi vii  See  Physic Vapours  Heart Humanity  HE that wants a heart wants every thing i  A wrong head may be convinced but who can give a Heart where it is wanting  ibid  The person who wants a feeling Heart wants the highest joy in this life i yet is saved many griefs by that defect i  Where the Heart in important cases involuntarily as may be said misgives its misgivings ought generally to be attended to as if the impulses of Conscience ii  It is more to a man's praise to shew a kind Heart than a cunning head ii  Persons of Humanity will not be ashamed on proper  occasions to shew by their eyes that they have feeling Hearts iv  Women should make it a rule to judge of the Heart of a man as he is or is not affected by the woes of others whether real or represented  ibid  He who can place his pride in a barbarous insensibility is ignorant of the principal Glory of the Human Nature  ibid  Who can be happy  says Lovelace  and have a feeling Heart yet he who has it not must be a Tiger and no Man iv v  Even those people who have bad Hearts will have a veneration for those who have good ones v  See also  iv vi  See also  v  What the unpenetrating world call Humanity is often no more than a weak mind pitying itself  Lovel  v vi  A capacity of being moved by the distresses of our fellowcreatures is far from being disgraceful to a manly Heart v vi  Sweet is the pain which generous natures feel for the distresses of others vi vii  A kind Heart is a greater blessing to its possessor than it can be to any other person who may receive benefit from it vi vii  See  Friendship Generosity Goodness  Honesty  WHAT a praise is it to Honesty that every man pretends to it even at the instant that he knows he means to be a knave i  Honesty is the chief pride of the low In the high the love of power of grandeur of pleasure mislead and induce a paramount pride which too often swallows up the more laudable one ii  What is there in this dull word or thing call'd Honesty  asks Lovelace  that even I cannot help thinking the temporary emanation of it in such a man as Tomlinson amiable iv v  It is so much every one's duty to be honest that no one has merit in being so every honest man therefore  may call himself honest without the imputation of vanity vii viii  See  Goodness  Human  Life  THE plainest path in our journey thro' life is  as acknowleges Lovelace  the safest and the best iii iv  In all human affairs the convenient and the inconvenient the good and the bad are so mingled that there is no having the one without the other iv  As Human Life is chequerwork a person of prudence will set so much good against so much bad in order to strike a balance iv  When can creatures who hold by so uncertain a tenure as that of Mortality be said to be  out of danger  vi  This is one of those common forms of speech that proves the frailty and the presumption of poor mortals at the same time  ibid  What are ten twenty or thirty years to look back to in the longest of which periods forward we shall all perhaps be mingled with the dust from whence we sprung vi vii  What is even the longest Life that in high health we wish for what as we go along but a life of apprehensions sometimes for our friends and oftener for ourselves vi vii  And at last when arrived at the old age we covet one heavy loss or deprivation having succeeded another we see ourselves stripp'd as may be said of every one we lov'd and exposed as uncompanionable poor creatures to the slights of jostling youth who want to push us of the stage in hopes to possess what we have  ibid  And superadded to all our own infirmities every day increasing of themselves enough to make the Life we wish for the greatest disease of all vi vii  To wish for an exemption from all infelicities were to wish for that which can never happen in this world and what perhaps ought not to be wish'd for if by a wish we could obtain it since we are not to live always make who is fond of prerogative and yet stands in need of the instruction which a man should be qualified to give i  The heart not the figure of the man is what should determine a woman in the choice of an Husband i  Sobriety in a man is a great point to be secured since so many mischiefs happen thro' excess i  As obedience is made a part of the matrimonial vow a woman should not teach a man by a failure in that to dispense with perhaps  more  material parts of his i  The principal views of a good Wife in adorning her person should be to preserve her Husband's affection and to do credit to his choice i  A married woman should be even  fearful  of attracting the eyes of any man but those of her Husband  ibid  A gloomy spirit in a Husband will swallow up a chearful one in his Wife i ii  Greatness of soul is required in a woman of sense and generosity to make her in her heart forbear to despise a lowminded Husband i iv ii iv  Husbands are often jealous of their authority and consequence with women who have wit ii ii  A Wife is the keeper of her Husband's honour ii iii  A Wife's faults in the world's eye bring more disgrace upon the Husband than even upon herself ii iii iii iv  The Wife by infidelity may do more injury to the Husband than the Husband can to the Wife ii iii  Handsome Husbands often make a Wife's heart ake iii  Handsome Husbands think the woman they marry under obligation to them ii  A Husband and Wife may be too much of one temper to agree iii  Two persons of tempers not comparatively bad may be very unhappy if they will be both out of humour at one time iii  It is a most affecting thing to be separated by death from a good Husband and left in destitute circumstances and that not by  his  fault iii  A wise man will rather endeavour to inspire a consciousness of dignity in the heart of his Wife than to depress and humble her in her own eyes iii iv  Prudence virtue and delicacy of mind in a Wife do a man more honour in the eyes of the world than the same qualities in himself iii iv  A good woman will be as delicate of her Husband's honour as of her own iv  A good Wife will think it her duty to lay up out of her own separate provision if not a too scanty one for the family good and for accidents iv  A tyrant Husband  says Lovelace  makes a dutiful Wife iv  The virtue of a woman who has a bad Husband is always is danger iv  A proud and bad spirit cannot bear a superiority of talents in a Wife tho' she and all her excellencies are his in full property iv v  A bountifultemper'd Wife should take care that by doing  more  than justice to others she does not  less  than justice by her husband iv v  To bear much with some Wives is to be under a necessity to bear more iv v  Husbands and Wives who live together in good understanding give to strangers an almost unerring proof of the goodness of their hearts v  Et è contra  vi  Et è contra    Happy is the marriage where neither Man nor Wife has any wilful or premeditated evil  or
