against  Judgment is infinitely worse than an error  in  Judgment i i  In order to form a Judgment of the tempers of men with whom we incline to have a close connexion we should attend to their behaviour upon slight disappointments or provocations and then we shall be able perhaps to decide what is to be ascribed to Art in them and what to Nature ii  She who acts up to the  best  of her Judgment at the time she is called upon to act has the less to blame herself for tho' the event should prove unfavourable ii  See also  i iii  See also  i  The eye and the heart when too closely allied are generally at enmity with the Judgment iii iii iv  To judge of the reasonableness of the conduct and resentment of others we ought to put ourselves exactly in their situations vi vii  Justice Injustice Right Wrong  IN an unjust donation the giver and receiver  the latter knowing it to be so  are both culpable i  There is a Right and Wrong in every thing let people put what gloss they will upon their actions ii  A woman may then doubt the Justice of her cause when those who loved her and are not principals in the point in debate condemn her iii  A man reflects upon himself and upon the company he has kept if he treats common instances of Justice Gratitude and Benevolence as extraordinary iii iv  Libertine as I am thought to be  says Lovelace  I never will attempt to bring down the measure of Right and Wrong to the standard of my own actions vi  Those who take advantage of the necessities of their fellowcreatures in order to buy any thing cheaper than the real worth are no better than robbers for the difference vi  There never was a woman so criminal who had not some to justify and side with her vii  In all Recommendations the good and convenience of  both  parties should be consulted vii viii in the note  If reflexions are justly thrown upon us we ought instead of resenting to profit by them vii viii  If unjust we ought to despise them and the reflecter too since it would be inexcusable to strengthen by anger an enemy whose malice might be disarmed by contempt vii viii  Justice no less than Mercy is an Attribute of the Almighty vii viii  K  Keepers Keeping  MEN who keep women as little know how to part with them as if they were married to them iii iv  A man may keep a woman but not his estate iii iv  Rakes who despise matrimony often become the dupes of lowbred women who govern them more absolutely than a wife would attempt to do  ibid  Keepers who are in possession of estates by legal descent will not wish that their fathers had despised matrimony as they do  ibid  Ought not Keepers to have the same regard for posterity as their fathers had  ibid  How can any thing be expected but riot and waste from creatures who know the uncertain tenure by which they hold and who have an interest quite different from that of their Keepers iii iv  Many considerations withhold a wife from infidelity to a man's bed that cannot weigh with a mistress iii iv  Men will bear many things from a kept mistress which they would not bear from a wife iii iv  Kept women who are generally lowborn low educated creatures can make no other returns for the partnership in a man's fortune into which they are lifted but the libidinous ones which a man cannot boast of but to the disgrace of both iii iv  A Keeper as he advances in years will find his appetite to Libertinism go off and that the regular familylife will be more and more palatable to him  ibid  Many considerations respecting himself and his illegitimate children should weigh with a man who keeps a mistress and despises wedlock iii iv  The man who is capable of fondness to his offspring and has a feeling heart will marry iii iv  The natural fruits of treading in crooked paths are dangers disgrace and a too late repentance  ibid  Keepers are often the cullies of their own Libertinism sliding into the married state with their wellworn doxies which they might have enter'd into with their ladies or superiors iii iv  See the remarkable story of Tony Jennings a noted Keeper ibid  And of Mr Belton and his Thomasine  iii iv  Old men imagining themselves under obligation to their young paramours seldom keep any thing from their knowlege iv v  A consuming malady and a consuming mistress  as in Belton's case  are dreadful things to struggle with in the last stage of life v vi vi vii  Hardly ever was there a Keeper that made not a Keeperess v vi  In the last stage of a Keeper's life the Mistress's more favoured gallant has been sometimes his Physician the dying man's Will has been ready made for him and Widow's weeds have been provided the moment he is departed in order to establish a marriage vi vii  See  Libertine  L  Law Lawyer  THE Law asserts not itself until it is offended i  Old Practisers in the Law value themselves too much for dispatch upon their skill as draughtsmen iv  The Lawyers who for the sake of a paltry fee undertake to make black white and white black endeavour  to establish iniquity by quirks and to rob the innocent iv v And are as base  Lovelace says  as his and old Sinclair's vile implement Dorcas v vi  The Law is a word that carries in it natural terrors to a guilty mind v vi  No wonder it should  says Lovelace  since those who will damn themselves to procure ease and plenty in the world must tremble at every thing that seems to threaten their methods of obtaining that ease and plenty v vi  It is but glossing over one part of a story and omiting another  says Lovelace  that will make a bad cause a good one vi vii  Learning  A LETTER'D education too generally sets the children of the poor above those servile offices by which the business of the world is carried on iii iv  Take the world thro' there are twenty happy people among the unletter'd to one among those who have had a schooleducation iii iv  Yet who would not wish to lift to some little distinction and genteel usefulness the person he desires to reward  ibid  The little words in the Republic of Letters like the little folks in a nation are the most useful and significant iv  A man of the deepest Learning may hear something from even a mean preacher that he knew not before or at least that he had not considered in the same light iv  The early Learning of women which chiefly consists in what they pick up from inflaming Novels and improbable Romances contributes greatly to enervate and weaken their minds vi vii  Libertine Rake  THE man wants but an opportunity to put in practice the crimes he is not ashamed to have imputed to him i  A Libertine Lover if preferred to a virtuous one is more likely to justify the  dislike  of his opposers than the  choice  of his favourer i  Rakes are more suspicious than honest men ii iii  Libertines by the frailty of those women they have triumphed over judge of the whole Sex ii iii  "  Once subdued and always subdued  "  is an article in the Rake's Creed ii iii iii iv  A Libertine who is a man of sense and knowlege must have taken great pains to suppress many good motions and reflexions as they arose in his mind or levity must be surprisingly predominant in it iii  The chief pleasures of a Libertine must arise from the pain the suspense the anguish of mind which he gives to the heart of a woman he pretends to love iii  A Libertine believes that no woman can be chaste or virtuous from principle iii iv  Every woman who favours a Libertine confirms him in his bad opinion of the Sex  ibid  If a woman loves a Libertine how will she bear the thought of sharing her interest in him with half the town and those perhaps the dregs of it  ibid  Prayers tears and the most abject submission are fuel to the pride of a Libertine  ibid  Fortunes squander'd estates mortgaged or sold and posterity robb'd are too often the result of a marriage with a Libertine iii iv  A Libertine familiarized to the distresses he occasions is seldom betrayed into a tenderness foreign to his nature iii iv  A Libertine will be more ashamed of shewing compassion by a weeping eye than of the most atrocious crimes iii iv  Libertines  as well as women love them  have not the ardors  Miss Howe says  that honest men have iii iv  Libertines are generally more severe exacters of implicit  obedience and rigorous virtue than other men v  No man who can think but of half the plagues that pursue an intriguing spirit would ever quit the foreright path v vi  A man who when old would enjoy in peace his own reflexions  Lovelace confesses  should never be a Rake v vi  The friendships and intimacies of Libertines are only calculated for strong life and health vi  What an ungrateful what an unmanly what a meaner than reptile pride is his whose delight is in the ruin of a person who confides in his honour and whom he ought to protect vii viii  Men of gallantry and intrigue are the instruments of Satan to draw poor souls into those subtle snares which at last will entangle their own feet vii viii  Libertines are infinitely worse animals than beasts of prey since these destroy thro' hunger and necessity only those from wantonness and sport vii viii  See  Advice  to Women  Courtship Cruelty Men find one another out as well as like Great ones  ibid  Little Spirits will always accommodate themselves to the tempers of those they want to work upon i ii  Grudging and narrow Spirits know not how to confer a benefit with that grace which gives the principal merit to a beneficent action i ii  One Meanness is not to be justified by another ii  To be afraid of Litle Spirits is to encourage insults iii  Meanness must ever be the portion of the man who is detected in acting vilely iv v  Tame Spirits will ever be imposed upon iv v  There is a malignancy in Little Minds which makes them wish to bring down the worthy to their own level vi vii  Nothing subjects the human mind to so much Meanness as the consciousness of having done wilful wrong to our fellowcreatures vi vii  People of narrow Spirits will praise generous ones because they find it to their purpose that all the world but themselves should be openminded vii viii  Narrowminded persons judging by their own hearts impute pride and ostentation to worthy persons as their motives to good actions vii viii  See  Covetousness Partiality Self  Love  THE Love which has not taken root deep enough to shoot out into declaration will not be brought forward by the blighting winds of anger or resentment i  Love takes deepest root in the steadiest minds i  Gratitude is not always to be construed into Love i  That Lion Love is not to be turned into a Lap dog i  Prodigies tho' they obtain our admiration never attract our Love i  Love to look back upon must appear to be a very foolish thing when it has brought a person born to affluence into indigence and laid a generous mind under obligation and dependence i  What is commonly called Love is a narrow circumscribed selfish passion and where the object of it is unworthy a passion too ignoble for a pure mind to encourage i  Pride and vanity are often the source of Love i  A person truly in Love will be wholly engross'd by one object i  Love will acquit where Reason condemns i  A prudent person will watch over the first approaches of Love i  It is a degree of impurity in a woman to love a sensual man i  Great encouragement must be given to Love to make it unconquerable i  Unrequited  or slighted  Love frequently turns to deepest hate i  Love delights to tame the lionhearted i ii  What a worse than Molochdeity is Love if it expects an offering to be made to its shrine of reason duty and discretion i ii  Love is a passion that often begins in folly or thoughtlessness and carried on with perverseness i ii  Love is as busy as a Monkey and as mischievous as a Schoolboy  says Miss Howe  i ii  Violent Love is a fervor like all other fervors that lasts but a little while i ii  Love is generally founded on mere notional excellencies i ii  Time and discretion will enable a woman to get over a first passion i ii  Love that deserves the name obliges the Lover to seek the satisfaction of the beloved object more than his own ii iii ii iii  True Love is ever accompanied with fear and reverence ii  A quarrel  says Lovelace  has sometimes its conveniencies in Love ii And more or less  adds he  all Lovers quarrel iii iv  Love is a fleeting thing little better than a name where morality or virtue does not distinguish the object of it ii  Silent awe the humble doubting eye and even the hesitating voice are the natural indications of true and respectful Love ii iii  True Love is fearful of offending ii iii  Weakness  Lovelace says  is the  true  name for Love ii iii  All the world is ready to excuse a fault owing to Love because all the world is apt to be misled by it  ibid  Love was ever a traitor to its harbourer  Lovel  ii iii  Love is not naturally a doubter ii iii  That avow'd Love which is follow'd by marriage however headstrong and indiscreet will have more excuses made for it than generally it ought to find iii  It is all over with  reasoning  Ladies  Lovelace says  when once Love gets into their heads iii  Platonic Love is Platonic Nonsense iii iv  A first passion thoroughly subdued often makes the man a rover the woman a tyranness iii iv  If Love is allowed to be an excuse for the most unreasonable follies what is meant by the doctrine of  subduing our passions  iv  What must be that Love which has not some degree of purity for its object  ibid  A  worthy  woman who consents to marry need not be urged explicitly to declare her Love iv  The proof of true Love is respect not freedom iv  Love is an encroacher Love never goes backward Nothing but the highest act of Love can satisfy an  indulged  Love iv  Love and Compassion are hard to be separated iv v  Love is seldom the friend of Virtue  Lovel  iv v  Love humanizes the fiercest spirits iv v  Love is a fire that if play'd with will burn the Lady can hardly ever esteem as a husband the man whom as a Lover she despises ii  How pleasantly can a false Lover pass his time while the gentle bosom of a Lady heaves with pity for his supposed sufferings for her ii  A blustering braving Lover cannot deserve encouragement ii  A Lover has not a right to be displeased with a Lady on her side of the solemnity ii  It is better for a Lady that her Lover should go away displeased with her than that he should leave her dissatisfied with herself ii  A generous Lover must seek to oblige the object of his love in every thing essential to her he be when a husband iii  Lovers chuse to be alone and are ashamed to have even a child present to witness to their foolish actions and more foolish expressions iv  See  Advice  to Women  Courtship EXALTED qualities may be sunk in a low and unequal Marriage i  A single Lady who can be brought but to balance on the change of her state may be easily determined by the glare and splendor of the iii  A man of spirit would not marry a Princess if he thought she but balanced a moment in her choice of him or of an Emperor  Lovel  iii  The man who knows it to be in his power to marry yet delays or resignedly leaves it to the woman to name the day is to be both suspected and despised iii  Marriage is the highest state of friendship If happy it lessens our cares by dividing them at the same clandestine correspondencies i  Policy as well as generosity will induce Masters and Mistresses to repose a confidence in their Servants ii  People in low stations have often minds not sordid  ibid  Take number for number there are more honest low people than high  ibid  Many Servants will scorn to deceive a confidence  ibid  That Servant cannot have sound principles who can allow herself to say talk of their Master's pedigree and descent with as much pride as if they were related to him iii v iii vi  Servants seldom keep their Master's secrets from one another be those secrets of ever so much importance to their Master iii  Servants are generally worse to have concerns with than their Principals iii  The greatest plagues people of condition meet with proceed from the Servants they take with a view to lessen their cares iv  Servants will be apt to take liberties with those Masters who employ them in a way that their duty will not warrant iv v  Servants united in one cause are intimate the moment they see one another iv v  They know immediately the kin and the kin's kin of each other tho' dispersed over the three kingdoms as well as the genealogies and kin's kin of those Minutenesses may be observed where greater articles are not neglected for them vi vii  Modesty Audacity  A MODEST person challenged will be diffident tho' innocent i  The Bold and Forward not being then they are useful and instructive thorns which by pricking the fingers of the too hasty plucker teach future caution vi vii  Difficulty gives poignancy to our enjoyments Those which are easily obtained generally lose their relish with us vi vii  The absent generally bear the load when the blame is apparently due somewhere vii  Actual distraction take it out of its lucid intervals must be an happier state than the state of suspense and anxiety which brings it on vii  Resolutions depending upon future contingencies are best left to future determinations vii viii  The greatest punishment that can be inflicted on us would often be the grant of our own wishes vii viii  Freewill enables us to do everything well while restraint and imposition make a light burden heavy vii viii  Oeconomy Frugality Housewifry  BY Frugality we are enabled to be both just and generous iii iv  Without Oeconomy no estate is large enough with it the least is not too small iv  The man who runs away from his accounts will in time be glad that he could run away from himself vi  Frugality is a necessary virtue niggardliness an odious vice vii viii  It is incredible what may be done by early rising and by long days well fill'd up vii viii  Persons who rise early and make good use of their  hours may be said to have lived more years at sixteen than some others at twentysix vii viii  Those who keep not a strict account seldom keep any vii viii  P  Palliation Evasion Excuse  A GOOD person will not palliate with a view to deceive ii  Artful Evasions are unworthy of a frank and open heart iii  It is no wonder that he who can sit down premeditatedly to do a bad action will content himself with a bad excuse vi  No Palliation ought to be made for wilful and premeditated vileness vii viii  Parents Children  SEVERITY in some cases is clemency i  Needless watchfulness and undue restraint often produce artifice and contrivance i  Parents by violently fighting against a Lover frequently fight for him i ii i ii  Daughters  says James Harlowe  are chickens brought up for a stranger's table i  Most unhappy is the situation of that  worthy  Child who is obliged in her own defence to expose a Parent's failings i  It is impolitic in Parents to join two people in one interest whom they wish for ever to keep asunder i  Tho' the parental authority should be deemed sacred yet Parents should have reason in what they do i  Where the heart of a Child is sought to be engaged the eye ought not to be disgusted i  A worthy Daughter would rather wish to appear amiable in the eyes of her own Friends and Relations than in those of all the world besides i  Disgraceful treatment will often bring about the very end which it is intended to frustrate i vii i viii  In family contentions when every expedient to bring about a reconciliation is tried whatever be the event the person so trying has the less to blame herself for i ii ii iii  How much greater must be the comfort of that young woman in an unhappy marriage who can reflect that she followed the advice of her Friends and owes not her unhappiness to her own headstrong will i ii i ii  The difference between the hard usage a Child receives from a severe Parent and the obsequious regard paid to her by a flattering Lover is enough to make her run all risks with the latter in order to get out of the hands of the former i  Parents sometimes make not those allowances for Youth which when young they wish'd to be made for themselves i ii  Parents must not always expect that  advice  should have the same force upon their Children as  experience  has upon themselves i ii  In giving advice and remonstrating Parents and Guardians should proceed by patient reasoning and gentleness that they may not harden where they wish to convince  ibid  Unkind circumstances on the Parents part and heedless ones on the Child's in a debate where both mean well will make small differences great ones  ibid  A Parent by forcing a Child to marry the man she hates may occasion an utter dissipation of the Child's morals and of consequence her everlasting perdition i ii  Aversion in a Child should be distinguished from wilfulness ii  Strings that are overstrained must either be relaxed or break ii  The time may come for a Child to consider as the highest benefit to herself those measures of a Parent which at present she may think grievous ii  The more obstinate a Child is in her opposition to a Parent's will the more will a Parent be apt to think his authority concerned to carry his point ii  Harsh and cruel treatment humbles a Child and makes her seem  cheap  in her own eyes ii  Is she not then in the way to become the easy prey of a man whom otherwise she would have despised  If Parents by appeals or otherwise needlessly expose a Child she will be apt to think that do what she will she cannot incur more disgrace than she already labours under ii  To endeavour to force a free mind is to dishonour it ii  It is better for a good Child to be able to say her Parents were unkind to  her  than that she was undutiful to  them  ii  See also  i iii  See also  i  The exertion of a seasonable lenity may save a penitent Child from utter destruction ii iv v vii iii iv vi vii  The Father and Mother who would secure to themselves the undivided love of their Children should avoid such durable contentions with each other as would distress their Children which side to take when they would be glad to reverence both iii  A good Parent must have greater pain in the  necessary  restraint of a headstrong Daughter than she can give to such a Daughter iii  At every age on this side matrimony it will be found that a Parent's wings are the most effectual safeguards of Daughters from the villainous birds of prey that hover round them iii  A Parent for a failure in her own duty is  not  answerable to her Child iii  Reverence is too apt to be forgot by Children when Parents forget what belongs to their own characters iii iv  Parents and Children when separated and seeing each other but seldom like other lovers shew their best sides to each other iii iv  The bad qualities in which fond Parents too often  indulge their Children when infants not seldom at riper years prove the plague of their hearts iv v  It is as necessary to direct Daughters in the choice of their female companions and to watch against the intrigues of womenservants as it is to guard them against the designs of men  Lovel  v  Parents the most indulgent in their own natures often from the errors of a Child incur the censure of hardheartedness v vi  Doubly faulty is that Child therefore who by a rash action not only disgraces herself but depreciates the most revered characters  ibid  What confusion of mind must attend the reflexions of a Child who from the most promising outsetting has brought ruin on herself and distress on her Friends  ibid  The voice of nature must at last be heard in favour of a Child truly penitent v vi vi vii  When a Daughter is strongly set upon a point it is better for a Mother if the point be of no high consequence to make herself of her party than violently to oppose her v vi  Parents should take care that they do not weaken their authority by a needless exertion of it v vi  What an enormity is there in that crime of a Child which can turn the hearts of Parents before indulgent against her vi  The resentment which Children and even the world may ascribe to cruelty in an offended Parent may be owing to excess of love and disappointed hopes vi  It is to be hoped  says Miss Howe  that unforgiving Parents were always good dutiful and passive Children to  their  Parents vi  Parents who would cure a Child's impatience of spirit should not betray a want of temper in themselves  ibid  Children depending on the weakness of their Parents tempers too ofen harden their own hearts vi vii  While Parents  think  a Child in fault as they have a  right to judge for themselves they ought to have great allowances made for them especially if till their displeasure took place they had always been kind and indulgent vi vii  Good Children make  both  their Parents happy in  each other  as well as in  them  bad Children unhappy in both vi vii  When the nearest Friends give up an unhappy Child every one is ready to propagate slander against her vi vii  A good Child will be careful of making a party against even harsh and severe Parents vi vii  It requires a high degree of understanding and discretion in a Daughter when grown up to let it be seen that she mingles reverence with her love to a Parent who has talents visibly inferior to her own vii viii  Parents in order to preserve their Children's veneration for them should take great care not to let them see any thing in their own conduct behaviour or principles which they themselves would not approve of in others  ibid  Such Parents as have a visible narrowness of heart must needs weaken their own authority with Children of spirit vii viii  See  Advice  to Women  Courtship Controul Duty Love Lover Marriage  Partiality Impartiality  MEN frequently give advice to others when consulted with an indirect view to something similar in their own case i  Goodwill or Love is often blind to real imperfections i  We are apt to praise our benefactors because they are  our  benefactors as if every body did right or wrong as they obliged or disobliged us i  We should endeavour to judge of ourselves and of everything that affects us as we may reasonably imagine others will judge of us and of our actions i  Were each person to tell his own story and to be believed there would not be a guilty person in the world i  No one should plead the errors of another in justification of his own i ii  Human nature sensible of its own defects loves to be correcting but chuses rather to turn its eye outward than inward iii  We often look into ourselves with a resolution not fairly to try but to acquit ourselves iii  It is difficult for a woman to subscribe to a preference against herself in lovecases tho' ever so visible iii  Poor arguments will do when brought in favour of what we like iii iv  An artful man bringing a case home to the passions or interest of his judges will be likely to succeed where he ought not iv v  That cause must be well tried where the offender takes his seat upon the same bench with the judge iv v  Whatever qualities we  wish  to find in one we love we are ready to find v  SelfPartiality is a dangerous misleader v vi  An impartial spirit having run into a punishable error will not forgive itself tho' its friends should forgive it v vi  Those least bear disappointment who love most to give it v vi vi vii  Many men are apt to take their measures of right and wrong from what they themselves  are  and cannot help  being  vi vii  So aukwardness may be a perfection with the aukward  ibid  It is difficult to go out of ourselves to give a judgment against ourselves and yet oftentimes to pass a just judgment we ought vi vii  Suffering persons are apt to be partial to their own cause and merits vi vii  It is far from being difficult for a  worthy  heart to reject  ject the man however once favoured whose actions it despises vi vii  See  Prepossession  Passions  THE command of her Passions was Clarissa's glory and is one of the greatest glories of the human mind i  The Manners and Passions of men and women are to be seen in miniature during their childhood i ii  If the irascible passions cannot be overcome how shall those be subdued to which bad habit joined to greater temptation gives stronger force ii  It is easy to make a passionate spirit answer all our views upon it ii  Turbulence and obsequiousness used in turn keep a woman's Passions alive and at last tire her into nonresistance  Miss Howe  iii  People in a Passion tho' within a few yards of each other hollow like travellers who are got out of their way and want to get into it again iii  How universally engaging it is  says Lovelace  to put a woman of sense in a Passion let the reception given to the ranting scenes in plays testify iii  Those Passions in women which they take no pains to subdue may have one and the same source  and tendency  with those which hurry on the headstrong and violent of the other Sex to the commission of the most atrocious crimes iii iv iii iv  Passion gives bodily strength Fear takes it away iii iv  Passion distorts the features and makes even an handsome person ugly iv  The passions of the gentle tho' slower to be moved than those of the quick are generally the most flaming when raised iv  It is both impudent and imprudent  says Lovelace  for a wife to be in a Passion iv  Passion and illwill are dreadful misrepresenters iv  Violence of Passion is too often admitted as a plea  at least as an extenuation  for violence and indecency of action both by the female sex and by the world iv v iv vi  To be able to arrest a woman's Passion in the height of its career  on an offence given to her modesty  is  says Lovelace  a charming presage iv v  A woman of a violent Spirit is often in more danger from an artful man than one of a steadier disposition v  Passionate women have high pulses  says Lovelace  and a clever fellow will make what sport he pleases with them v vi  Who can account for the workings and ways of a passionate and disappointed woman  Lovel  vi  Passion has different ways of working in different bosoms as humours or complexion induce vi  The Passions of the Female Sex if naturally drawn will distinguish themselves from the masculine Passions by a softness that will shine thro' rage and despair vi vii  See  Anger Violent Spirits  Patience Impatience  PERSONS unaccustomed to controul are impatient of controul i  If afflictions are sent for corrective ends Impatience may lead into more punishable errors ii  An impatient spirit subjects itself to deserved humiliation ii iii  When a point is clear and selfevident it is difficult to find Patience on being obliged to enter into an argument in proof of it iii  See also  i iii  See also  i  Patience and perseverance are able to overcome the greatest difficulties iii iv  No man ought to be impatient at imputations he is not ashamed to deserve iv v  An innocent man  will not  be outrageous upon reports made to his disadvantage a guilty man  ought not  iv v  The injured has a right to upbraid the injurer ought to be patient v vi  Persons who by their rashness have made a breach in their duty should not enlarge it by their impatience v vi  Impatience is generally the child of selfpartiality v v  The person who is employed as a mediator should not be himself overready to take offence vi  People new to misfortune are often too easily moved to impatience vi vii  It is not just for two friends more than for man and wife to be out of Patience at one time vi vii  In a deep distress a man of an impatient spirit is apt to think that every face and even the face of nature should wear the marks of that woe which affects him vii  Pedants Colleges  YOUTHS raw from the Colleges are not fit prescribers to the gentler Sex   Physic Physicians  PUNISH  and  prescribe  synonymous terms in Physic iv  Why  asks Lovelace  when Physicians can do no good will they not study to gratify rather than nauseate the palate of their both to study and industry  ibid  A worthy Physician will pay a regular and constant attendance upon his patient watching with his own eyes every change and every new symptom of his malady vii viii  He will vary his applications as indications vary  ibid  He will not fetter himself to rules laid down by the fathers of the art who lived many hundred years ago when diseases and the causes of them as also the modes  of living and climates and accidents were different from what they are now vii viii  He should not be greedy of fees but proportion his expectation of reward to the good in his conscience he thinks he does  ibid  See  Health Vapours  Pity Mercy  PITY is a good preparative to Love i  We should shew Mercy or Lenity to unhappy persons whose calamities in a like situation might have been our own i  Disgraces brought on persons by themselves ought not to be pitied i  In our attendances on a dying person we pity him for what he suffers and we pity ourselves for what we must one day in like manner suffer and so are doubly affected ii  The Pity which a rash child often meets with when she has brought upon herself an irreparable evil should generally be tranferred to her parents and friends iii iv  Pity from one often begets Pity from another whether the occasion for it be either strong or weak iii iv  God wants not any thing of us for Himself He enjoins us works of mercy to one another as the means to obtain  his  mercy v vi  The brave and the wise know both how to pity and excuse vii viii  See  Generosity Goodness Magnanimity  Politeness Travelling  POLITENESS necessary to the support of life since three parts in four of them if it were would not know how to obtain that  much  ii  Poverty is the mother of health  ibid  The pleasures of the Mighty are obtain'd by the tears of the Poor ii  The man who is used to Poverty and can enjoy it not aiming to live better tomorrow than he does today  and did yesterday is above temptation unless it comes cloathed to him in the guise of truth and trust  Lovel  iii  Were it not for the Poor and the Middling  Lovelace says  the world would deserve to be destroyed iii  Common  or  bred  beggars should be left to the public provision iv  In the general scale of beings the lowest is as useful and as much a link of the great chain as the highest vii viii  Power Independence  EVERY one more or less loves Power i  Yet those who most wish for it are seldom the fittest to be trusted with it i vii i vii  An honest man would not wish to have it in his Power to do hurt iv  Power is too apt to make men both wanton and wicked iv v iv v  If our Power to do good is circumscribed we shall have the less to answer for iv v  People who have money or Power never want assistants be their views ever so wicked v vi  Who that has it in his Power to gratify a predominant passion be it what it will denies himself the gratification of it  Lovel  vi vii  Both Sexes too much love to have each other in their Power vii is commended if already it has them  not i  See also  i  and  v ii  See also  i  and  vi  How soothing a thing is Praise from the mouth of those we love i ii  Would every one give Praise and Dispraise  only  where due shame if not principle would mend the world iii  It is a degree of affectation to decline joining in the due Praise of our children because they are our own iii iv  Those who are accustomed to Praise will not be proud of it iii iv  A person too fond of Praise is apt to be misled by it iv  Those are generally most proud of Praise who least deserve it iv  Praise reproaches when applied to the undeserving iv  Praise will beget an emulation in a generous mind to deserve or to continue to deserve it v vi  Those who praise with warmth the laudable actions of another where they themselves are not benefited may be supposed to have a spirit like that which they applaud vii  See also  i viii  See also  ii  Persons who find themselves heard with applause ought to take care that Goodness Merit Virtue  Prejudice Prepossession Antipathy  EARLYBEGUN Antipathies are not easily eradicated i  Those we dislike can do nothing to please us i ii i ii  An extraordinary Antipathy in a young Lady to a particular person is generally owing to an extraordinary prepossession in favour of another i  An eye favourable to a Lover will not see his faults thro' a magnifying glass ii  Prepossession in a Lover's favour will make a Lady impute to illwill and prejudice all that can be said against him  ibid  Old prejudices  tho' once seemingly removed  easily recur ii iii  To those we love not  says Lovelace speaking of Mr Hickman  we can hardly allow the merit they should be granted vi  Prejudices in  disfavour  generally fix deeper than Prejudice in  favour  vi vii  Whenever we approve we can find an hundred reasons to justify our approbation and whenever we dislike we can find a thousand to justify our dislike vi viii  See  Love Lover  Pride  PRIDE in people of birth and fortune is not only mean but needless i  Distinction and quality may be prided in by those to whom it is a  new  thing  ibid  The contempt a proud great person brings on himself is a counterbalance for his greatness  ibid  It is sometimes easier to lay a proud man under obligation than to get him to acknowlege it i ii  Pride ever must and ever will provoke contempt i ii  There may be such an haughtiness in submission as may entirely invalidate the submission ii  A person who distinguishes not may think it the mark of a great spirit to humour his own Pride even at the expence of his politeness ii  It is to be feared there are more good and laudable actions owing to Pride than to Virtue ii  Pride and meanness are as nearly allied to each other as the poets tell us wit and madness are ii  Nothing more effectually brings down a proud spirit than a sense of lying under pecuniary obligation ii iii  Pride when it is native will shew itself sometimes in the midst of mortifications iii  Pride frequently eats up a man's prudence iii iv  Pride is an infallible sign of weakness or something wrong either in the heart or head or in both iii iv  It is possible for a woman to be proud in supposing she has no Pride iv  We ought not to value ourselves on talents we give not to ourselves iv vii iv viii  How contemptible is that Pride which stands upon diminutive observances and gives up the most important duties iv  Some women have from Pride what others  more laudably  have from principle The Lord help the Sex  says Lovelace  if they had not Pride v  Pride or Arrogance invites mortification v vi  Haughty spirits when they are convinced that they have carried their resentments too high frequently want but a good excuse to condescend vi vii  Pride in man or woman is an extreme that hardly fails sooner or later to bring forth its mortifying contrary vi vii  Persons of accidental or shadowy merit may be proud but inborn worth must be always as much above conceit as i  Those who want the fewest earthly blessings most regret that they want any  ibid  Riches are valuable in that they put it in our power to confer favours on the deserving i ii  Success in unjustifiable devices often sets bad people above keeping decent measures ii  In great Prosperity as well as in great Calamity we ought to look into ourselves and  fear  ii  Success has blown up and undone many a man ii iii  Who is there that Wealth does not mislead iii  Prosperity sets up merit as a mark for envy to shoot its shafts at iii iv  The greatly Prosperous bear controul and disappointment with difficulty iv  Great acquirements are great snares  ibid  Those are generally most proud of Riches or Grandeur who were not born to either iv  Success in projects is everything Those schemes will appear foolish even to the contriver of them which are frustrated and render'd abortive v vi  Prosperity and independence are much to be coveted as they give force to the counsels of a friendly heart v vi  People may be too rich to be either considerate or contented vi vii  A life of Prosperity is dangerous in that it affords not the trials which are necessary to wean a person from a world that such will find too alluring vii viii  Providence  WHAT have we to do but to chuse what is right to be steady in the pursuit of it and leave the issue to Providence i  It is more just to arraign ourselves or our friends than Providence iii iv  The ways of Providence are unsearchable vii viii  Various are the means made use of by Providence to bring sinners to a sense of their duty  ibid  Some are drawn by love others are driven by terrois to that divine refuge  ibid  See  Insolence Pride  Prudence Wisdom Discretion  THE trials of the Prudent are generally proportioned to their Prudence i  Prudent persons will not put themselves in the power of a servant's tongue i  Prudence will oblige a woman to forbear complaining or making an appeal against her husband i  Deeds not words will be the only evidence to a prudent person of a good intention i ii ii  A prudent woman who is addressed by a man of suspected virtue tho' hopeful of the  best  will always in doubtful points be fearful of the  worst  ii iii  We are often fatally convinced of the vanity of  mere  human Prudence iii iv  A prudent and good person who has been a little misled will do all in her power to recover as soon as possible her lost path iii iv  To avoid the supposed disgrace of retractation a prudent person will be backward to give her opinion in company of persons noted for their superior talents iii iv  A wise woman despising the imputation of prudery on one hand and coquetry on the other will form her conduct according to what her own heart tells her of the  fit  and  unfit  and look upon the opinion of the world as matter only of secondary consideration iii iv  Prudent persons will not need to be convinced by their  own  misfortunes of the truth of what common experience daily demonstrates vi vii  Difficult situations are the tests of Prudence and Virtue vi vii  It is a happy art to know when one has said enough vii viii  Prudent persons will always leave their hearers wishing them to say more rather than to give them cause to  shew by their inattention and uneasiness that they have said too much vii viii  See  Advice  to Women  Goodness Generosity Merit Virtue Conviction Conversion  A MAN can hardly be expected to reform who resolves not to quit the evil company he has been accustomed to delight in i  Pretences to instantaneous Convictions are to be suspected i  Conviction is half way to amendment i  To reform by an enemy's malevolence is the noblest revenge in the world i  Very few Convictions arise from vehement debatings i ii  The first step to Reformation is to subdue sudden gusts of passion and to be patient under disappointment ii  The most abandon'd of Libertines generally mean one day to reform ii iii  Should they not therefore even as Libertines resolve against atrocious guilt were it but to make their future compunction less pungent    Reformation cannot be a sudden work ii iii  There is more hope of the Reformation of a man of sense than of a fool ii  See also  i iii  See also  i  But this is a delusive hope and has been the cause of great mischief for who thinks not the man she loves a man of sense The observations that follow are nearer the truth and deserve to be well considered  A man who errs with his eyes open and against Conviction is the worse for what he knows iii  The man of parts and abilities who engages in a baseness knowing it to be so is less likely to be reclaimed than one who errs from want of knowlege or due Conviction v vi  Women think that the reclaiming of a man from bad habits  as Lovelace himself observes  is a much easier task than in the nature of things it can be v vi  For Mr Belford's scheme of Reformation see Vol  vii  to  viii  p    Little hope can there be of reclaiming a man who is vile from premeditation vi  To what a bad choice is many a worthy woman betray'd by that false and inconsiderate notion raised and propagated no doubt by the author of all delusion  That a reformed Rake makes the best Husband  Belf vii viii  Little do innocents think what a total revolution of manners what a change of fixed habits nay what a conquest of a bad nature and what a portion of divine grace is required to make a profligate man a good husband a worthy father and a true friend from PRINCIPLE vii viii  It is a high degree of presumption for a woman to suppose her own virtue so secure as that she may marry a profligate in hopes to reclaim him vii viii  The sincerity of that man's Reformation is hardly to be doubted who can patiently bear being reminded of his past follies and when he can occasionally express an abhorrence of them vii viii  See  Goodness Religion Repentance  Relations  To borrow of Relations is to subject one's self to an inquisition into one's life and actions  Lovel  ii iii  Religion Piety Devotion Sabbath  A GOOD man will not easily be put out of countenance  by scoffers  when the cause of Virtue and Religion is to be vindicated i ii  There are men who think themselves too  wise  to be religious ii  There is something beautifully solemn in Devotion  says even Lovelace  iii  The Sabbath  says he  is a most excellent institution to keep the heart right  ibid  It is a fine sight  adds he  to see multitudes of wellappearing people all joining in one reverent act an exercise how worthy of a rational being  ibid  If as religion teaches us we shall be judged in a great measure by our benevolent or evil actions to one another what must be the condemnation of those who have wilfully perpetrated acts of the most atrocious violence upon their innocent fellowcreatures iv v  Libertines are generally for making a Religion to their practices  a wickedness which nevertheless Lovelace disclaims  v vi  Religion will teach us to bear inevitable evils with patience v vi  Altho' I wish not for life  says Clarissa  yet would I not like a poor coward desert my  post  when I can maintain it and when it is my duty to maintain it vi  I will do every thing I can  continues she  to preserve my life till God in mercy to me shall be pleased to call for it vi  Religious considerations timely enforced will prevent the heart from being seized with violent and fatal grief vi  Disappointments may bring on an indifference to this life but a truly pious resignation to death requires a better and deeper root vi  Enthusiasts often depreciate the Scriptures they mean to extol by abused and indiscriminate applications vi vii  Even a Lovelace disclaims as ill manners jesting upon religion or religious men vi vii  A person of innate piety cannot think of shortening her own life whatever her calamities may be even by  neglect  much less by  violence  vi vii  Our best prayer in affliction in doubtful or critical situations is That God's will may be done and that we may be resigned to it vi vii  Religion is the only refuge of a heart labouring under heavy and unmerited calamities vi vii  Religion enjoins us not only to forgive injuries but to return good for evil and Clarissa blesses God for enabling her to obey its dictates vi vii  Persons of Piety cannot permit resentment passion or anger to appear or have place in the last disposition of their secular affairs vi vii  God will have no rivals in the hearts which he sanctifies vii  Persons of Education and Piety will distinguish themselves as such even in their anger vii viii  It is a great mistake to imagine that Piety is not entirely consistent with good nature and good manners vii viii  Religion if it has taken proper hold of the heart is  says Lovelace  the most chearful countenancemaker in the world  ibid  Sourness and moroseness indicate but a noviceship in Piety or Goodness  Lovel ibid  See  Goodness Virtue  Remorse  THE troubles of the injured are generally at an end when the injury is committed but when the punishment of the injurer will be over who can tell  Lovel  v  How often  says Lovelace  do we end in occasions for the deepest Remorse what we began in wantonness v  The Remorse that is brought on merely by disappointment cannot be lasting v vi  Nothing  says Lovelace  but the excruciating pangs which the condemned soul feels at its entrance into the eternity of the torments we are taught to fear can exceed what I now feel and have felt for this week past vii  What a dreadful thing is afterreflexion upon a perverse and unnatural conduct vii viii  Heavy must be the reflexions of those who on the loss of a worthy friend have acts of unmerited kindness to that friend to reproach themselves with vii viii  Repentance Contrition  WHAT is it that men propose who put off Repentance and Amendment but to live to  sense  as long as sense can relish and to reform when they can sin no longer iii  That Contrition for a guilt under which the guilty till detected was easy is generally to be ascribed to the detection and not to a due sense of the heinousness of the guilt v vi  Repentance I have a notion  says Lovelace  should be set about while a man is is good health and spirits v vi  What is a man fit for  not a new work surely  when he is not himself nor master of his faculties  Lovel ibid  Hence as I apprehend it is that a deathbed repentance is supposed to be such a precarious and ineffectual thing  Lovel ibid  As to myself  proceeds he  I hope I have a great deal of time before me since I intend  one day  to be a reformed man v vi  Lovelace lived not to repent  I have very serious reflexions now and then yet am I afraid of what I was once told that a man cannot repent when he willNot to hold it I suppose is meantI have repented by fits and starts a thousand times  Lovel  v vi  Laugh at me if thou wilt  says Belford  but never never more will I take the liberties I have done but whenever I am tempted think of Belton's dying agonies and what my own may be vi vii  The most hopeful time for Repentance is when the health is sound when the intellects are untouched and  while it is in a person's power to make some reparation to the injured or misled vi  See also  ii vii  See also  iii  Reparation should always follow Repentance vi vii  That Repentance for a wrong step which precedes the suffering that follows it must generally be wellgrounded and happy vii viii  Repentance to such as have lived only carelesly and in the omission of their regular duties is not so easy a task nor so much in their power as some imagine vii  See also  v viii  See also  v  No false colouring no glosses does a truly penitent man aim at vii viii  See  Remorse Religion  Reprehension Reproof Correction  THE Reproof that savours more of the cautioning friend than of the satirizing observer always calls for gratitude i  Reproofs to be efficacious should be mild gentle and unreproaching ii iii  How much more eligible is it to be corrected by a real friend than by continuing either blind or wilful to expose one's self to the censure of an envious and perhaps malignant world iii  The correction that is unseasonably given is more likely to harden or to make an hypocrite than to reclaim iii  A bad man reprehends a bad man with a very ill grace iv v iv vi  Persons reprehending others should take care that altho' they may not be guilty of the faults they condemn they are not guilty of others as great iv  The benevolence of our purpose should be very apparently seen in all our Reprehensions vii viii  See  Censure  Reputation  THE man who is careless of his Reputation must be so either from an abandoned nature or from a consciousness that he deserves not the world's good opinion i  It is just that a man should bear to be evilspoken of who sets no value upon his Reputation i  The man who has been always chary of his Reputation has an excellent security to give to a woman for his good behaviour to her iii iv  See  Men  and Women  Resentment  PERSONS who have carried their Resentments too high are not easily brought to retract or forgive i  If an injury be not wilfully done or avow'd to be so there can be no room for lasting Resentment i ii  The man who would resent as the highest indignity the imputation of a wilful falshood ought surely to be above the guilt of one i ii  The presence even of a disliked person takes off the edge of Resentments which absence frequently whets and makes keen ii  Women who when treated with indecency have nothing to reproach themselves with may properly resent v vi  Resentment and revenge ought ever to be separated v vi  That Resentment which is express'd with calmness and without passion is most likely to last vi  Passion refuses the aid of expression sometimes where the Resentment  prima facie  declares expression to be needless vii viii  See  Anger Passion Revenge  Respect Reverence  PERSONS who deserve Respect will meet with it without needing to require it i  Persons who would exact Respect by an haughty behaviour give a proof that they mistrust their own merit and seem to confess that they  know  their actions will not attract it i  Familiarity destroys Reverence but not with the prudent the grateful and the generous ii  Persons in years expect the Reverence due to their years yet many of them having not merit are ashamed of the years which can only intitle them to Reverence ii  A studied Respectfulness or complaisance is always to be suspected iii v  See also  ii iii vi  See also  iii  Even a wicked man will revere a woman that will withstand his lewd attempts iv v  It shall ever be a rule with me  says Miss Howe  that he that does not regard a woman with some degree of Reverence will look upon her and sometimes treat her with contempt vi  See  Advice  to Women  Courtship Love Men  and Women  Revenge  REVENGE grafted upon disappointed love is generally the most violent of all our passions i  The highest Revenge a low female spirit can take is to prevent her rival's having the man she loves and procuring her to be obliged to marry the man she hates i  Even the ties of relationship in such a case lose all their force i  Revenge will not wipe off guilt i  What Revenge can be more effectual and more noble than a generous and well distinguished forgiveness vii viii  See  Resentment  S  Satire  TRUE Satire must be founded in good nature and directed by a right heart ii  When Satire is personal and aims to expose rather than to amend the subject of it how tho' it were to be  just  can it be useful ii v ii vi  Friendly Satire may be compared to a fine lancet which gently breathes a vein for health sake the malevolent Satire to a broad sword which lets into the gashes it makes the air of public ridicule ii  See  Anger Passion Resentment  Secrets Curiosity  NOTHING flies faster than a whisper'd scandal iv v  Listeners are generally conscious of demerit iv v  It becomes not a modest man to pry into those secrets which a modest man cannot reveal iv v  People who mean well need not affect Secrets iv v  Few people who are fond of prying into the Secrets of others are fit to be trusted iv v  Overcurious people will whisper a Secret about till it becomes public in the pride of shewing either their consequence or sagacity  ibid  Health and spirits  but not discretion or decency  allow busy people to look out of themselves into the affairs of others v vi  Secrets to the prejudice of the innocent ought not to be kept v vi  There may be occasions where a breach of confidence is more excuseable than to keep the Secret  Lovelace  vii  See also  Vol V p viii  See also  vi  I believe I should have kill'd thee at the time if I could  says Lovelace to Belford  hadst viii  See  Observations General  Self SelfInterest Selfishness  WHAT is the narrow Selfishness that reigns in us but relationship remember'd against relationship forgot i  SelfInterest and Ambition too often cut asunder the bonds of relationly love i  It is in the power of the slightest accident to blow up and destroy the longreaching views of the Selfish i  A man's own interest or convenience is a poor plea if there be no better on which to found expectations of favour from another i  The address which is persisted in against the undoubted inclination of the beloved object is too selfish to be encouraged i  What a low selfish creature must that child be who is to be rein'din only by the hope of what a parent can or will do for her i  The selfish heart never wants an excuse for not doing the  good  it has no inclination to do ii  It is very low and selfish to form our judgments of the general merits of others as they are kind or reserved to ourselves ii  There must be great Selfishness and meanness in the love of a man who can wish a young creature to sacrifice her duty and conscience to oblige him ii iii iii  The man who has no other plea for a woman's favour but that of his loving her builds only on a compliment made to her SelfLove by his Selfishness iii  To serve onesself and punish a villain at the same time is serving both public and private  Lovel  iii iv  Selflove will most probably give those who advise with us on their most intimate concerns an interest in our hearts whether they deserve it or not iii iv  Self is a grand misleader iv  Those men or even that body of men who prefer  their private interest to the public are unworthy members of society v  Self is an odious devil that reconciles to some people the most cruel and dishonest actions vi  See  Covetousness Partiality  Sensuality  THE less of soul there is in man or woman the more sensual are they iv  Love gratified is love satisfied and love satisfied is indifference begun  Belford  iv  This deified passion in its greatest altitude is not fitted to stand the day iv  Shall such a sneaking passion as sensual love be permitted to debase the noblest  ibid  See  Love Lovers  Sickness Infirmities  GREAT allowances ought to be made for the petulance of persons labouring under illhealth i  When peoples minds are weakened by a sense of their own infirmities they will be moved on the slightest occasions v vi  A sick person tho' hopeless of recovery should try every means that is properly prescribed to her for the satisfaction of her friends both present and absent v  Sickness palls every appetite and makes us loath what we once loved vi  When Sickness comes free livers look round them and upon one another like frighted birds at the sight of a Kite just ready to souse upon them vi  Sickness enervates the mind as well as the body vi vii  A long tedious Sickness  says Lovelace  will make a bugbear of any thing to a languishing heart vi vii  An active mind tho' clouded by bodily illness cannot be idle vi vii  Travelling is undoubtedly the best physic for all those  disorders which owe their rise to grief or disappointment vii  See  Adversity Health Physic Repentance Vapours  Suspicion Doubt Jealousy  A PERSON who labours hard to clear herself of a fault she is not charged with renders herself suspectable i  Persons who have been dipt in love themselves are the readiest to suspect others i ii ii iii  Suspicion Watchfulness Scolding  Miss Howe says  will not prevent a daughter's writing or doing any thing she has a mind to do ii iii  When we doubt of a person's sincerity we should observe whether his aspect and his words agree ii iii  Where Doubts of any person are removed a mind not ungenerous will endeavour to make the suspected person double amends iii  Jealousy in a woman is not to be concealed from woman if both are present and in love with the same man iii  Constitutional  Jealousy preys not on the health iii iv  Jealousy in a woman accounts for a thousand seemingly unaccountable actions  Lovel  iv v  See  Apprehension Love Parents  and Children  T  Tears  BEAUTY in Tears is beauty heighten'd iv v  Anatomists  says the hardhearted Lovelace  will allow that women have more watry heads than men v vi  Nothing dries sooner than Tears  Lovel  v vi  The man is to be honour'd who can weep for the  distresses of others and can such a one be insensible to his own vi vii  Tears ease the overcharged heart which but for that kindly and natural relief would burst vi vii  Tears are the prerogative of the human creature  ibid  It cannot be a weakness to be touch'd at great and concerning events in which our humanity is concern'd  ibid  See  Beauty Cruelty Eyes Heart  Theory  KNOWLEGE by Theory is a vague uncertain light which as often misleads the doubting mind as puts it right iv v  The knowlege that is obtained by Theory without experience generally fails the person who trusts to it v  Theory and practice must be the same thing with a truly worthy person vi vii  Thoughtfulness Sensibility  A THOUGHTFUL mind is not a blessing to be coveted unless it has such a happy vivacity join'd with it as may enable a person to enjoy the present without being overanxious about the future ii  A thoughtful woman who has given her lover an undue power over her will be apt to behold him with fear and look upon herself with contempt ii iii  The difference which such a one will find in the looks and behaviour of her lover will very soon convince her of her error  ibid  The finer Sensibilities make not happy iii  Some people are as sensible of a scratch from a pin as others are from a push of the sword vi vii  See  Heart  Tyranny  IT is an high act of tyranny to insist upon obedience to an unreasonable command iii  Tyranny in  all shapes  is odious but  Fathers  and  Mothers  who are Tyrants can have no bowels iii iv  The woman who beforehand behaves to a man with Tyranny will make a poor figure in a man's eyes afterwards  Mrs Howe  iii iv  Call Tyranny an ungenerous pleasure if thou wilt  says Lovelace  softer hearts than mine have known it Women to a woman know it and  shew  it too whenever they are trusted with power iv v  See  Husband  and Wife  Parents  and Children  Reflexions  on Women  V  Vanity Conceit Affectation  A VAIN man will be apt to construe to his advantage any  particularity  shewn him by a lady mean by it what she will i  The person who is vain of  exterior  advantages gives cause to doubt his  interior  i  The outside of a vain man generally runs away with him i  Some persons are not able to forego the ostentation of sagacity tho' they sacrifice to it the tenderness due to friendship and charity ii  Men who have a Conceit of their own volubility love to find ears to exert their talents upon ii iii  Men of parts may perhaps think they have a privilege to be vain yet they have the least occasion of any to be so since the world is ready to find them out and extol them ii iii  The man who is disposed immoderately to exalt himself must despise every body else in proportion iii  Men vain of their learning and acquirements parading with one another before the other Sex may probably have women present who tho' sitting in smiling silence may rather despise than admire them iii  The man who in conversation takes  knowingly  the wrong side of an argument shews Vanity in the high compliment he pays to his own abilities iii  The man who wants to be thought wiser or better or abler than he is does but provoke a scrutiny into his pretensions which seldom ends to his advantage iii iv  He that exalts himself insults his neighbours who are then provoked to question even the merit which otherwise might have been allow'd to be his due  ibid  A too great consciousness of superiority often brings on contempt iii iv  Old bachelors when they like a woman frequently think they have nothing to do but to  persuade themselves  to marry iii iv  Affectation will make a woman seem not to understand indecent freedoms of speech in men but modesty if the freedoms are gross will make her resent them iv  It is generally the conscious overfulness of Vanity or Conceit that makes the vain man most upon his guard to conceal his Vanity  Lovel  iv v  Opinionative women are in danger when they meet with a flatterer who will magnify their wisdom in order to take advantage of their folly  Lovel  v  Selfsufficiency makes a weak person the fittest of all others for the artful and designing to work upon v vi  An openmouth'd Affectation to shew white teeth  Lovelace considers  as an invitation to amorous familiarity v vi  The darkest and most contemptible ignorance is that of not knowing one's self and that all we have and all we excel in is the gift of God vii viii  See  Heart Human  Nature  Men  and Women  Vapours  VAPOURISH people are perpetual subjects for physicians to work upon  Lovel  iv  Lowspirited people are the physical tribe's milch cows  Lovel  iv  Vapourish people draw out fearful bills of indictment against themselves  Lovel  iv  If persons of low spirits have not real unhappiness they can make it even from the overflowings of their good fortune iv vi v vii  The mind will at any time run away with the body vi  The mind that busies itself to make the worst of every disagreeable occurrence will never want woe vi vii  The distempers we make to ourselves and which it is in our power to lessen ought to be our punishment if we do not lessen them vi vii  See  Health Physic  Veracity Truth  THOSE persons have profited little by a long course of heavy afflictions who will purchase their relief from them at the expence of their Veracity iv  It is presumed  that no man ever ruined a woman but at the expence of his Veracity iv  A departure from truth was hardly ever known to be a  single  departure iv v  Were I to live a thousand years  says Clarissa  I would always suspect the Veracity of a swearer v vi  How glorious is it for a child to be able to say with Clarissa that she never to the best of her knowlege told her mother a wilful untruth vi vii  I have never lyed to man  says Lovelace  and hardly ever said Truth to Woman the first is what all free livers  cannot  say the second what every Rake  can  vii viii  See  Advice  to Women  Courtship Love Lover Vows  Violent Spirits  VEHEMENT and obstinate Spirits by tiring out opposition will make themselves of importance i  People who allow nothing will be granted nothing i  Those who aim to carry too many points will not be able to carry any  ibid  We are too apt to make allowances for such tempers as early indulgence has made uncontroulable ii  If a boisterous Spirit when it is  under obligation  is to be allowed for what were the tables to be turn'd would it not expect  ibid  Too great allowances made for an impetuous Spirit are neither happy for the person nor for those who have to deal with him  ibid  Providence often makes hostile Spirits their own punishers ii  While a gentle Spirit will suffer from a base world a violent one keeps imposition at distance iii  Imposing Spirits and froward Spirits have a great advantage over courteous ones iii  Violent Spirits provoked will quarrel with the first they meet iii iii iv  Violent Spirits want some great sickness or heavy misfortune to befal them to bring them to a knowlege of themselves vii  The man who is violent in his resentments when he thinks himself right would oftener be so but for that violence vii viii  He is guilty of great injustice who is more apt to give contradiction than able to bear it vii viii  Impetuosity of temper generally brings on abasement  ibid  See  Anger Insolence Passion Pride Prosperity Resentment Revenge  Virtue Virtuous Principle  WHAT a mind must that be which tho' not virtuous itself admires not virtue in another i  No woman can be lovely that is not virtuous ii  If persons pretending to Principle bear not their testimony against unprincipled actions what check can they have ii  In a general corruption a stand must be made by somebody or Virtue will be lost And shall it not be  I  will a worthy mind ask who shall make this stand ii  Provocations and temptations are the test of Virtue iii ii  Honours next to divine are due to a woman whose Virtue is superior to trial or temptation ii iii  Lively women seldom know the worth of a virtuous man ii iii  Sound Principles and a good heart are the only bases on which the hopes of a happy future with respect to both worlds can be built iii iv  The Virtue of a woman tried and approved procures for her not only general respect but a higher degree of love when proved even from the tempter v vi  A virtuous woman will conquer her affection for a man who is capable of insulting her modesty v vi  What virtuous woman can submit to make that man her choice whose actions were and ought to be her abhorrence vi  See  Generosity Goodness Innocence Merit Magnanimity Modesty Prudence Purity  Vivacity  PERSONS of active spirits and a pleasurable turn seldom take pains to improve themselves i  Lively talents are oftener snares than advantages i  That is a happy Vivacity which enables a person to enjoy the  present  without being anxious about the  future  ii  Persons of Vivacity do not always content themselves with saying what they think  may  be said but to shew their penetration or sagacity will indulge themselves in  saying all that  can  be said on a subject ii  It is difficult for persons of lively dispositions so to behave as to avoid censure v vi  It is impossible to share the delights which very lively spirits give without partaking of the inconveniencies that will attend their volatility vi  Vows Curses Oaths Promises Protestations  A PROMISE ought not to preclude better consideration ii  What must be that man who would be angry at a woman whom he hopes one day to call his wife for dispensing with a rash Promise when she is convinced it was rash  ibid  The Vows of a maiden may be dispensed with by her Father when he hears them Num xxx  ibid  In like manner the Vows of a wife may be dispensed with by her husband  ibid  Could the Curser punish as he speaks he would be a fiend ii iii  The Almighty gives not his assent to rash and inhuman Curses iii  To pray for those that curse us is to perform a duty and thereby to turn a Curse into a blessing iii  The man that is very ready to promise is seldom equally ready to perform iii  It is a shame for grown persons to have frequent need to make promises of amendment iii iv  The most immaculate Virtue is not safe with a man who has no regard to his own honour and makes a jest of the most solemn Vows and Protestations iii iv  One continued string of Oaths Vows and Protestations varied only by time and place fill the mouth of a libertine v vi  Men who gain their dishonourable ends by perjuries  no less prophane and defy heaven than deceive and injure their fellowcreatures v vi  The man who binds his Promises by Oaths indirectly confesses that his word is not to be taken v  See also  i vi  See also  ii  Is it likely that he who makes free with his God will scruple any thing that may serve his turn with his fellowcreatures v vi  The assertions of a libertine who is not allow'd to swear to what he avers will lose their principal force  Lovel  vi vii  Those men who are most ready to resent the Lye given them by a  man  least scruple generally to break the most solemn Oath to a  woman  vi vii  See  Advice  to Women  Courtship Libertine Love Lover Veracity  W  Widow  IT is ill trusting to the discretion of a Widow whose fortune is in her own hands iii iv  That Widow is far engaged who will quarrel with her child for treating with freedom the man who courts herself iii iv  A Widow's refusal of a lover is seldom so explicit as to exclude hope iii iv  The Widow who wants nothing but superfluities is easily attracted by those gewgaws that are rare to be met with  ibid  Widows should be particularly careful with whom they trust themselves at public entertainments and parties of pleasure v  To be a Widow in the first twelve months is  Lovelace says  one of the greatest felicities that can happen to a fine woman vi vii  See  Reflexions  on Women  Wills Testators Executors  c  No testator that can avoid it should involve an Executor in a Lawsuit vi vii  It ought to be a Testator's study to make his Executors work as light as possible vi vii vii viii  Of all last Wills those of monarchs are generally least regarded vii viii  Survivors cannot more charitably bestow their time than in a faithful performance of an Executorship vii viii  This last act ought not to be the last in composition or making but should be the result of cool deliberaand as is more frequently than justly said of a sound tion mind and memory which too seldom are to be met with but in sound health vii viii  When a Testator gives his reasons in his last Testament for what he wills all cavils about words are obviated the obliged are assured and those enjoy the benefit for whom the benefit was intended vii viii  I have for some time past  says Clarissa  employ'd myself in putting down heads of my last Testament which as reasons offer'd I have alter'd and added to so that I never was absolutely destitute of a Will had I been taken off ever so suddenly vii viii  The first reading of a Will where a person dies worth any thing considerable generally affords a true test of the relation's love to the deceased vii viii  What but a fear of death a fear unworthy of a creature who knows that he must one day as surely die as he was born can hinder any one from making his last Will while he is in health vii viii  Persons in making thier last Wills should consider the  pleasure  as well as the  ease  of their Executors and not put a generous man upon doing what would give him pain vii viii  Wit Talents Conversation  THERE is no glory in being proud of Talents for the abuse of which a man is answerable and in the right use of which he can have no merit  Lovel  i  Men who make a jest of sacred or divine institutions  would often forbear if they did not think their Licentiousness  Wit  ii  Wit with gay men is one thing with modest women another iv  That cannot be Wit that puts a modest woman out of countenance iv  There is not so much Wit in wickedness as Rakes are apt to imagine iv  The Wit of Libertines consists mostly in saying bold and shocking things with such courage as shall make the modest blush the impudent laugh and the innocent stare iv  See also  i iv  See also  i  Men who affect to be thought witty are apt to treat the most serious subjects with levity vi  Freelivers are apt to mistake wickedness for Wit vi  All the little nibblers in Wit whose writings will not stand the test of criticism make it a common cause to run down critics vi vii  Many things in conversation occasion a roar of applause when the heart is open and men are resolved to be merry which will neither bear repeating nor thinking on afterwards  Belf  vii viii  Common  things in the mouth of a man we admire and whose Wit has pass'd upon us for sterling become in a gay hour  uncommon  ibid  See  Imagination  Writers  THE inflaming descriptions of Poets and Romancewriters often put a youthful mind upon the scent for an object to exert its fancy upon  In other words  Often create beauty and place it where nobody else can find it i  Romancewriters never forget to give their Heroine a Cleanthe a Violetta a Clelia or some such prettynamed confidante an old nurse at least to help her out at a dead lift ii  Unnatural similies drawn by poetical lovers to illustrate beauty rather depreciate than exalt it ii iii  A person may not be a bad critic tho' not himself a very excellent Writer iii  Our poets  Mr Belford says  hardly know how to create a distress without horror murder and suicide and think they must shock your souls to bring tears from your eyes vi vii  Female words tho' of uncertain derivation have generally very significant meanings vii  Early familiar Letterwriting is one of the greatest openers and improvers of the mind that man or woman can be employed in vii viii  It is to be lamented that many eminent Writers who are capable of exalting virtue and of putting vice out of countenance throw away their time upon subjects merely speculative disinteresting and unedifying vii viii  The ingenious authors of pieces of a light or indecent turn which have a tendency to corrupt the morals of youth to convey polluted images or to wound religion are dishonest to their own talents and ungrateful to the God who gave them those talents  ibid  Y  Youth  LITTLE inducement has an headstrong Youth to correct a temper which gives him consequence at home i  Young persons should be careful in giving advice to a young friend in cases where passion and prudence are concerned i ii  Young persons whose minds are not engaged by acts of kindness and condescension will be put upon contrivances ii  Youth is the time of life for imagination or fancy to work in A Writer therefore who would wish to please a judicious eye will lay by his works written at that time till experience shall direct the fire to glow rather than blaze out ii  Youth not qualified to judge for itself is often above advice ii  Young folks are sometimes very cunning in finding out contrivances to cheat themselves ii iii ii iii  It is a most improving exercise as well with regard to style as to morals to accustom ourselves early to write down every thing of moment that befalls us iii  There is not so much bravery in youthful choler as young men imagine iv  In company where there are strangers it is right for young gentlemen who would wish to be thought well of to hear every one speak before they allow themselves to talk vi vii  See  Duty Education Learning Wit Writers  A COLLECTION OF THE Moral  and
