innocent fashions of the times will sit ungracefully upon a man iii iv  When the novelty of any change of condition is over the principal pleasure is over and other novelties are hunted after to keep the pool of life from stagnating iv  Public appearances whether at court or church on a marriage  Lady G says  are a compliment made to fine cloaths and jewels at the expence of modesty  ibid  Fashion tho' called decorum has often beat modesty out of the house  ibid  In the article of personal appearance propriety and degree as well as Fashion should be consulted v vi  Singularity is usually an indication of something wrong in judgment  ibid  See  Good Man Good  Wife  Modesty Prudence Politeness Public  Places The  World  Female  Dignity  How can men expect that delicacy from the sex which is their ornament and distinction if they hold women cheap and treat them with indignity ii iii  A generous man for the honour of the sex will be concerned if he is in such a situation as obliges him to decline proposals made to him by the friends of a lady who honours him with her esteem  ibid  Ladies who speak favourably of a man in his absence who forms not pretensions upon them would perhaps soon convince that man of his mistake were his presumption to rise upon their declared good opinion of him ii iii  The woman who declares her love of a good man to proper persons  because  of his goodness does more honour to her own sex than to the other  ibid  The man who is not a friend to the sex in general must be unworthy of a good mother and good sisters  and have fallen into bad company nor deserves to have been favoured with better ii iii  Let not the want of cultivation of the intellects of women induce a man of letters to hold the sex cheap iii  The cause of virtue and the sex can hardly be separated  ibid  It is a grateful thing to all women to have a man in love whether with themselves or not because a man by it recognizes the dignity and power of the sex iii  Politeness as well as gratitude will ever prompt a generous man to acknowlege a woman's favour to him as a condescension iii  Female Delicacy expects to be argued with courted persuaded iv v  A woman tho' she happens to be inferior in birth and fortune to the man who addresses her should retain a Dignity that shall set her above either insult or contempt iv v  The young woman whose duty and inclination were never divided will dignify the choice of a man of the highest fortune and merit v vi  What a princess in the eye of all her friends will the declared love of a polite and good man make a deserving young woman How will his affection for her consequence with everybody v vi  See  Advice  to Women  Courtship Education Generosity Good Man Modesty  Single  Women  Femalities  WOMEN who have several lovers like women in a mercer's shop distracted with the variety of his rich wares often choose the worst and reject the best i  ibid    Female pride like love tho' hid under a barrel will flame out of the bung  Mr Selby  i  ibid    The love of admiration swallows up the hearts and souls of women  Mr Selby    ibid  There are points in which all women agree and make a common cause of them i  ibid    It is a sign  says Sir Rowland Meredith  when women are desirous to conceal their age that they think they shall be good for nothing when in years i  ibid    Women can do no less than reward a man by their smiles who makes himself a monkey to divert them i  ibid    Women's eyes frequently run away with their understandings i  ibid    Marriage like Aaron's rod often swallows up female friendship  ibid  The fault of the women in the present age is that they will hardly stay till they are asked yet consider not that men value nothing highly but what they obtain with difficulty i  ibid    It is an easy thing to alarm a woman on the side of her vanity i  ibid    Women and painters make visitors who admire them welcome  ibid  Women are so much in love with compliments that rather than want them they will compliment one another yet mean no more by it than the men do i  ibid    Love secrets are generally the cement of female friendships  ibid  The Devil's at home  a phrase that seems to be verified by the practice of the modern women i  ibid    A woman cannot be guilty of a meaner pride than that of seeing a number of men in her train i  ibid    The man  says Sir Thomas Grandison  who argues with a woman on points in which nature and not reason is concerned must follow her through a thousand windings yet at last be beat out of the course ii  Folly in women  says the same gentleman  is a native of the soil a very little watering will make it sprout and choak the noble flowers which education has planted ii  I never in my life  says he  knew a woman who was wise by the experience of others  ibid  The blind God  adds he  often sets women on a paceing beast they amble prance parade upon it till the r heads turn round and then they gallop over hedge and ditch leap fences and duty decency and discretion are trodden under foot ii  Matrimony and liberty is a girlish connexion ii  Giddy women seldom doubt a man who doubts not himself tho' they should the more suspect him for his audacity ii  The man who knows how to say agreeable things to a woman has her vanity on his side since to doubt his veracity would be to question her own merit  ibid  Women where love and their own happiness interfere are the most incompetent judges of all others ii  What must be the woman who makes contrivances necessary to induce her to do a right a kind an obligeing thing ii iii  A woman who hopes to encrease her consequence by appearing indifferent to the addresses of a man she likes deals with him as common buyers and sellers do with petty chapmen ii iii  Women by perseverance may outteaze if they cannot outargue the wisest man ii iii  The Female eye expects to be gratified whence men of appearance often succeed when men of merit fail iii  Women by their little affectations in their loveaffairs frequently gratify their own punctilio only to a penetrating eye they discover by them what they wish to conceal iii  Where is the sense of a woman's parading with a worthy man of whose affection she has no reason to doubt and whose visits she allows iii  All women from the grandmother to the granddaughter giving the stories of their loves affect to have it thought they were very difficult to be won whether th y were so or not  Some women act by their lovers as if they thought coyness and modesty the same thing iii  Others as if they were sensible that if they were not insolent they must drop into the arms of a lover on his first question iii  Handsome men may attach giddy women without saying a single word iii  Women do not often fall in love with philosophers iii  An humoursome woman is not always so much to be blamed as her mother iii iv  Women whether in courtship or not dislike not vivacity in a man iii iv  Some of them will be better pleased with an innocent freedom than with profound respect  ibid  Angry women are formidable only to those who are afraid of their anger or who make it a serious thing  ibid  A man of common penetration may easily see to the bottom of a woman's heart a cunning woman cannot hide it a good woman will not the difficulty lies in her not knowing her own mind iii iv  Women designed to be dependent as well as meek creatures when left to their own wills often know not what to resolve upon  ibid  Women are so used to courtship that they know not how to do right things without itNor always with it iii iv  What a tormentress can that woman be who can vex a husband yet keep her own temper iii iv  Women love not to be prescribed to even in the points to which they are not naturally averse iv  And for this very reason  says Lady G  because it becomes them to submit to prescription  ibid  I believe  adds she  if my good man wished me to stay at home I should torture my brain as other good wives do for inventions to go abroad  ibid  The sex  says Signor Jeronymo  never know their minds but when they meet with obstacies to their wills iv v  Our sex  says Lady G  is a foolish sexToo little or too much parade Yet were it not that we must be afraid to appear forward to the man himself we should  treat the opinion of the world with contempt v iv  Women who aim at overdelicacy and are solicitous to take their measures from the judgment of those without them generally behave like simpletons  Lady G  v vi  Pragmatical souls  adds she  form their notions of what ought to be a man's behaviour in courtship either on what they have read or by the addresses to themselves of some favourite silly fellow who perhaps was equally aukward and unmeaning tho' in a weak hour he appeared to them the flower of courtesy  ibid  Wise or foolish before we are all equally foolish when in love  says the same Lady  the same froward petulant captious babies occasionally and did not the same idle passion make men as great fools as ourselves they would hardly think us worthy of their pursuit v vi  We women  says Lady G  must have something to find fault with in a good man v vi  Women generally like not a man the less for finding something to mend in him v vi  Women  Mr Selby says  are but the apes of one another v vi  There is a time in every woman's life in which the eye rather than the judgment is the director of her heart v vi  We women  says Lady G  are afraid of a wise man No wonder therefore that we seldom choose one when a fool offers v vi  Nor is  adds she  a prudent man a favourite with us whence so many unhappy marriages v vi  For the man who is prudent or but suspected to be so in love is guilty of an heterodoxy in the eyes of women The very word and thing called prudence is excluded from the female notion of love v vi  We women  says Lady G  are foolish creatures in our love affairs and know not what is best for ourselves  i id  A  l women more or less perhaps from the brilliancy of their imaginations are romancers  ibid  Young women every one loving to raise a dust to shew her significance are apt to look upon a state of tranquillity as a state of insipidity  ibid  Women love power yet seldom know how to make a right use of it v vi  Women sometimes by their very reserves betray their expectations v vi  A proud woman should be above owing obligation to her man for bearing with her foibles v vi  You maidens  says Lady G in the wantonness of her vivacity  are generally poor proud pragmatical mortals you profess ignorance but in heart imagine you are at the tiptop of your wisdom v vi  True female resignation lies only in words v vi  The first vice of the first woman was curiosity and it runs through the whole sex v vi  Women whether weakly or robust are hardly ever tired with dancing on joyful occasions On such they will tire the men some few of those excepted who like themselves are brought up to be idle and useless  Lady G  v vi  A petulant woman is always increasing the number of her obligations to those who bear with her vi vii  When a woman's eye leads her choice imagination can easily add all good qualities to the plausible appearance vi vii  Opposition or resistance is the soul the essence of all sorts of heroism vi vii  The words  constancy  and  perverseness  are often synonymous terms when used of Heart is a criterion of innocence and goodness ii  Why should young ladies be ashamed to own a love for a worthy object when proper persons for motives not ungenerous make enquiries ii  Frankness of Heart demands equal frankness iii  That may be an affectation in one company that may be but a necessary reserve in another iii  The men are their own enemies  says Miss Byron  if they wish women to Affectation Concealment Love Lover Ingenuousness Men  and Women  Modesty Single  Women  Friendship Friend  FRIENDSHIP and Reserve are incompatible i  ibid    Confidence engages confidence i  ibid    Kindred minds will find out and assimilate with each other i  ibid    The tenderness shewn to us by friends who would keep from us the full knowlege of an unhappy event when we suspect all is not as we wish is often as painful to us as would be the most explicit communication i ii  Our imaginations are hereby set at work and moreover the strength of mind and discretion supposed in the concealer and the weakness in that of the person concealed from sometimes carries with it an appearance of insult  ibid  The person who is consulted for his advice shoul  have no retrospection to himself that might in the lea  affect the consulter i ii  Love may be selfish but Friendship that deserves the name cannot ii  The man who considers himself as the  first  person in a Friendship in cases that may hurt the other should  be reminded that he judges as meanly of the understanding as of the justice of that other ii  No motive of Friendship can justify a wrong action ii iii  Those who have a claim upon us for our Friendship should not be called  other  people ii iii  Friendship is the balm and seasoning of life ii iii  A man who is capable of Friendship in the true meaning of the word cannot be defective in any of the social duties  ibid  No Friendship can be held with a man who asking advice is angry with his friend for speaking his mind ii iii  We may love a Friend with all his faults but should not be blind to them iii  Friendship ought not to byas against justice however dear to us may be the one party however indifferent to us the other iii  Friendship cannot be kept with a young man who is under the dominion of dissolute companions and will not allow of remonstrances in cases that concern his morals iii  What a solitariness what a gloom what a darkness must possess the mind that can trust no friend with its inmost thoughts iii  The essence of Friendship is communication mingling of hearts and emptying our very soul into that of a true friend  ibid  People of condition have more flatterers than friends about them iii  The Friendship of a good man is a credit to every one whom he honours with it iii iv  Disinterested Friendship is the basis of true love iii iv  The word  Friend  ought not to be polluted by affixing ideas to it that cannot be connected with it iii iv  True Friendship is a delicate union of like minds that exalts the human nature  ibid  There may be love which though it has no view but to honour yet even in wedlock ripens not into Friendship  ibid  An open and generous heart will not permit a cloud to hang long upon the brow of a Friend without enquiring into the reason of it in hopes to be able to dispel it iv v  An abatement in the freedom of a Friend is a charge of unworthiness upon one's self and ought to be obviated the moment it is observed  ibid  True Friendship being disinterested and more intellectual than Love is nobler than that deified passion iv v  That Friendship is only pretension which on proper calls exerts not itself in action iv v  A prudent man when he lends his assistance to his distressed Friend aims only at practicable and legal not romantic redress v  The Friendship of a good man makes life desirable v vi  A generous man knows not  self  when justice and the service of his Friend stand in opposition to it  ibid  A worthy and modest man will be thankful for advice and will be ready to doubt the love of the Friend who gives him cause to question his friendly freedom with him vi vii  Friendship will at pleasure make a safe bridge over seas it will cut an easy passage thro' rocks and mountains and join together distant countries vi vii  Kindred souls are always near  ibid  See  Duties  Moral and Religious  Good Man Ingenuousness Love Magnanimity Modesty Prudence  G  Gaming Gamesters  WHO but a man's self ought to suffer by his own rashness or inconsideration iv v  Successful Gamesters who before they play'd were possessed of fortunes which would have enabled them to answer the stakes they play'd for had they been losers have some plea to make to the loser  ibid  If the loser would have exacted payment from them  had he been a winner he ought not to complain if they are rigorous to him iv v  The man who calls in the laws of his country to his aid when he has been a loser will have this benefit that he can never again be seen in the same company  ibid  A man distressed by Gaming ought to take care that he becomes not himself one of the very men he has so much reason to wish he had avoided  ibid  And that he permit not creditors from valuable considerations to suffer by him  ibid  What honest man would not rather be the sufferer than the defrauder  ibid  What a diabolical nature must that man have who having been ruined himself will endeavour to draw in other men to their ruin  ibid  A just man will divest himself of his whole fortune if necessary for the satisfaction of his creditors and live within the pittance their generosity will allot him for subsistence iv v  And this not only for justice sake but were his difficulties owing to his own inconsideration as a punishment for it  ibid  To what pity can a man pretend who will put to hazard a certainty in hope of obtaining a share in the property of others  ibid  Strange that a man should be so infatuated as to put on the cast of a die the estate of which he is in unquestioned possession from his ancestors iv v  Yet who will say that he who hopes to win what belongs to another does not deserve to lose his own  ibid  A loser at play will have the less reason for regretting the unhappy situation to which he has reduced himself if his losses bring him to a right sense of his folly iv v  General  Observations  AN unworthy man promoted runs away with the reward due to the worthy i  ibid    By the softness or harshness of the voice some judgment may be made of the heart and manners of a woman  says Sir Rowland Meredith  i  ibid    There are many bad wives who would have been good ones had they not married to their dislike i  ibid    Good beginnings are necessary to good progresses and to happy conclusions i  ibid    The love of two worthy people for each other is a a proof of the goodness of both their hearts i  ibid    Disg  sts and affections cannot always be reasonably accounted for i  ibid    An honest man must appear in every light with such advantages as will make even singularity agreeable i  ibid    Bad habits are sooner acquired than shaken off i  ibid    How many things must a man do in an exigence who knows not what is right to be done i  ibid    Persons who are ready to apologize for themselves before they are accused are to be suspected i  ibid    What straws do we catch at to save our drowning hopes of the recovery of a dying friend while life continues i  ibid    Singularity is a fault to which great minds are too often subject i ii  Those who have least to do are generally the busiest people in the world i ii  A man should always mistrust himself when inclination is strong i ii  Alarming spirits love not to be alarmed ii  Subjects may go off happily in conversation which will not bear recital ii  The wives and daughters of citizens too generally are the apes of the gentry ii  There would be no supporting life were we to feel quite as poignantly for others as we do for ourselves ii  Boistrous spirits whether fathers husbands or masters are generally most observed ii  Persons of eminent abilities seldom err in small points ii  The merchants of Great Britain are the most useful members of the commonwealth ii  One weakness as one crime is frequently the parent of another ii iii  Those men who are the readiest to give offence are generally when brought to the test the most unfit to support their own insolence ii iii  The epithets  pretty young little  are great softeners of harsh words ii iii  Whispers in conversation are no more to be heard than asides in a play ii iii  Disingenuous persons do rash things and try to find an excuse for them afterwards iii iv  Fear will make cowards loving iii iv  A disappointed lover does not easily fix again iii iv  Different means may be taken to arrive at the same end iii iv  Great fortunes are great snares iii iv  The man who builds a merit on his civility shews that it is not natural to him iv  When we have taken a liking to any person we are very solicitous that he should think equally well of us iv  It is no bad sign in a faulty person to retain a reverence for the good iv  It is generally the way of those who intend not to amend to set their hearts against their admonishers iv v  It often happens that the man who was once most likely to be happy in a near alliance with a respectable family is on the frustrating of his hope looked upon and for  that  reason as the most remote from its friendly love iv v  We are often punished by the grant of our own wishes v  There are circumstances in which we think our behaviour  was not right yet know not in what it was wrong v vi  Goodnatured men will often concede when they are not convinced v vi  The mind can be but full It will be as much filled with a small disagreeable occurrence having no other as with a large one v vi  The English populace are honest goodnatured worthy and shew themselves to be so when persons of good character attract their admiration by a public appearance on any solemn occasion or engage their pity by an undeserved distress v vi  We know not presently how to frame our lips to new names or new titles v vi  Bashful people are always increasing their own difficulties v vi  It is much easier to find fault with others than to be faultless ourselves  ibid  The respectful or slighting behaviour of poor neighbours as a man of condition passes by them will give the general character of such a one vi vii  The impending evil is always the most dreaded tho' the avoiding of that frequently leads to a much worse vi vii  An easy heart will give a very different appearance to prospects which from an uneasy one seem dark and cloudy vi vii  In almost every thing we act but upon general probabilities One exception out of a thousand ought never to determine us vi vii  Very few of us poor mortals know what is best for ourselves vi vii  See Miscellaneous  Observations  Generosity OverGenerosity  WHAT a consciousness of inferiority fills a generous mind when it labours under the sense of obligations it cannot return i  ibid    A generous man will not wish for the power of circumscribing a generous mind ii  Prudence is the measure of Generosity  ibid  A man cannot be generous if he be not just ii  A generous man will delight in giving pleasure to others but it ought not to be expected in instances that would give pain to himself ii iii  A generous spirit will not insult the fallen or disgraced however faulty they may have been ii iii  Free minds bear not to be ungenerously dealt with ii iii  Generosity which is an higher grace than even justice will not confine itself to obligations either written or verbal iii  True Generosity has no mixture of pride or insolence in it iii iv  Were policy  only  to be consulted a character for Generosity and Goodness is worth obtaining iii iv iv v  A generous man values riches principally as they enable him to lay an obligation and exempt him from the necessity of receiving one iii iv  A generous mind will be as ready to confer as to receive a benefit iv  A disinterested and generous man is born a ruler iv v  How imperfect will a finespirited man think that happiness which he cannot enjoy without giving pain to another iv v v vi  A generous mind will be pained to receive presents which it knows not how either to deserve or return iv v  A generous man will remember that he has two hands to one tongue he will use the latter to declare that  both  the former are at the service of a friend in distress iv v  Princes are not above asking money of their people as freegifts on the marriage of their children v vi  He that would act more greatly than a prince may before he is aware be less than a gentleman v vi  A man disposed to act nobly and to sacrifice his own  interest on particular occasions will meet with generous restraint from a man of spirit v vi  A generous man will be thought a weak man if he submit to imposition v vi  A generous man on being preferred by a lady who has several lovers will not forbear pitying those whose disappointment is owing to his happiness v vi vi vii  The lady on such an occasion must if worthyminded find a gentle sigh arise in her bosom whether she sees the lovers she has refused at usual places or if they forbear to come where they were accustomed to see or meet her  ibid  A generous mind will not accept of all that is offered by a generous mind vi vii  A generous mind may be led into error but when it knows it to be error it will not continue in it vi vii  See  Beneficence Good Man Gratitude Magnanimity Modesty  Girls  GIRLS who among themselves have a new set of company to talk over and when a new admirer is one of them are not apt to break off conversation abruptly i  ibid    A very young wife often makes a vapourish mother ii  Girls ought not to marry before they have done growing  ibid  An early bloom in girls is not to be wished for ii iii  There is as much difference in Girls as in fruits with regard to their maturing  ibid  When Girls begin to look out for admirers their friends had better be aforehand with them if possible than to leave them to purvey for themselves iv  To give a Girl consequence with herself and to seem to repose a confidence in her discretion is often a proper way to make her act discretely  ibid  Parents should not too long treat as Girls those daughters who have found out that they are women and who are treated as such by others  Lady G thus sets forth the beginning and progress of love in Girls  Young Girls  says she  finding themselves vested with new powers and a set of new inclinations turn their staring eyes out of themselves v vi  They imagine that they must receive as a lover the first single man that simpers at them  ibid  Then they return downcast for ogle that he may ogle on without interruption  ibid  They are soon brought to answer letters which confess flames the writer's heart never knew  ibid  The Girl doubts not either her gifts or her consequence She is more and more beautiful in her own eyes as he more and more flatters her  ibid  Or if she does doubt of her own perfections being so high as he seems to think them she questions not his sincerity and she has heard a thousand instances of the mighty power of love transforming plainness into beauty in the eye of a lover  If her parents are  a  verse the Girl is  per  verse and the more the less discretion there is in her passion  ibid  She adopts the word  constancy  She declaims against  persecution  She calls her idle flame love which only was a something she knew not what to make of a cupidity that like a wandering bee had it not settled on this flower would on the next whether bitter or sweet v vi  Love  adds this lively lady  is a word very happily at hand to help giddy creatures to talk with and look without confusion of face on a man who for the sake perhaps of her fortune only tells her a thousand falsehoods  ibid  Love is a passion that is generally confined to the days of girlhood Even Girls in love would laugh at a woman who was violently stung with that passion after she was turned of honest thirty or was at years of discretion vi vii  See the articles  Daughters magnetism in Goodness Bad people indeed will find out bad people to associate with in order to keep one another in countenance but they are bound together by a rope of sand ii iii  While trust confidence love sympathy by a reciprocation of beneficent offices twist a cord which bind good men to one another and cannot easily be broken  ibid  Goodness is essential to true happiness ii iii  Goodness and Greatness are synonomous terms iii  Goodness is an uniform thing and will alike influence every part of a man's conduct iii  A good man will not value himself on his ancestry alone iii  Honour should be paid to men for better reasons than either for their riches or nobility iii  A man cannot be good if not uniformly so iv v  The man who loves a young woman for the sake of her Goodness gives distinction to himself vi vii  See  Duties  Moral and Religious  Good Man Modesty Prudence  Good Man Man  of Honour  Honest Man  HONESTY is good sense politeness amiableness all in one i  ibid    A Man of Honour qualifies not in points of veracity be the occasion either light or serious i  ibid    A good man tho' above singularity will be governed  by the laws of reason and convenience rather than by the fashion i  ibid    Vice is a coward when it knows it will be resolutely opposed i  ibid    What has a good man unavoidably engaged in a right cause to fear i  ibid    Grandeur of air accompanied with ease and freedom of manners good breeding accessibility are qual fications in a man that will attract the general love and reverence i  ibid    The good sense of a really fine gentleman is not rusted over with sourness or moroseness  ibid  He is above quarrelling with the world for trifles  ibid  But still more above making such compliances with it as would impeach either his honour or conscience  ibid  He will live to himself and to his own heart and make the approbation of the world matter but of second consideration with him  ibid  He will not be misled either by false glory or false shame the great snares of virtue  ibid  Men of truly great and brave spirits are generally humane tender merciful i  ibid    While men of base and low minds are usually tyrannical cruel insolent where they have power  ibid  Deviations which in common men will be deemed slight are not to be excused in men of exemplary characters i  ibid    A good man will not be brought to disavow a right measure i  ibid    A man who resolves to pay a sacred regard to laws divine and human has no reason to fear a wicked man i ii  A Man of Honour would not marry a princess did he not think she preferred him to all other men i ii  A good man where either truth or justice is concerned will not palliate i ii  Occasion calls not out every man equally to an exertion of great and amiable qualities  ibid  The man who can subdue his passion and forgive a  real  Injury is a hero i ii  A wise man will not  seek  danger But when he cannot  avoid it he will consider the occasion as a call upon him for fortitude i ii  Intrepidity in danger is sometimes the means of extricating a man from it i ii  A man who is not a timid man has more reason than one who is to be afraid of being provoked by insult or affront i ii  A Good Man will honour him who lives up to his religious profession whatever it be  ibid  A Good Man will not engage even in a national cause without examining the justice of it i ii  Extraordinary merit has some forfeitures to pay ii  To make his enemies his friends to put wicked men into a way of reformation and to make it a bad man's interest to be good are happy incidents in the life of a worthy man ii  Equivocation as little becomes the mouth of a worthy person as a downright falsity that of a less worthy man ii  What a glory as well in its influences as in itself belongs to goodness ii  An honest man fears not a scrutiny into his conduct ii  Superior excellence like sunshine brings to light those spots and freckles in inferior worth on comparison which were hardly before discoverable ii  A Good Man will not stoop to flatter any one and least of all the great and the rich ii  It becomes a good man in some cases to soften the severity of his virtue ii  A Good Man will have a large charity but will not extend it to credulity ii  A worthy man will allow a third person when questions arise to sit in judgment upon his actions ii  Goodness must love goodness ii  A Good Man will not suffer the narrowness of other people's hearts to contract his own ii  A man who has made a right use of the power intrusted to him is amply rewarded in the consciousness of having so done ii  A worthy man will make it his study as far as his power reaches to raise the hearts of such deserving persons as inevitable calamities have made spiritless ii  A Good Man will look upon every accession of power to do good as a new trial of the integrity of his heart ii  A Good Man will not be guilty of false modesty which breaking out into singularity would give the suspicion of a wrong direction in cases where it may be of use to suppose a right one ii iii  A Good Man lives to his own heart He thinks it not good manners to slight the world's opinion tho' he will regard it only in the second place ii iii  A Man of spirit and goodness will not by his complaisance countenance the enormities of the great ii iii  A Good Man will be ready to do what is right without being compelled to do it by law ii iii  A truly beneficent man having power will aim at amending the hearts as well as fortunes of his friends and dependents ii iii  A benevolent man will not wish to hold in his hands the power tho' he were not to exert it of distressing the heart of a worthy man whom he has obliged and whose honour he distrusts not ii iii  A Good Man must have difficulties to encounter with by which a man of the world would not be embarrassed ii iii  How much more glorious a character is that of the friend of mankind than that of the conqueror of nations ii iii  The life of a good man however unfashionable the doctrine is a continual warfare with his passions iii  A Good Man tho' he will value his own countrymen yet will think as highly of the worthy men of every nation under the sun iii  A Man of Honour will deem his good name his riches his integrity his grandeur princes to him are only princes as they act iii  A good man is a prince of the Almighty's creation iii  The public has a superior claim in the abilities of a wise and good man iii iv  Where there are two lights in which the behaviour of any person may be set a good man will always choose the most favourable iii iv  A Good Man has no demands upon his estate or fortune but those of reason and therefore will have it in his power to do generous things iii iv  The heart of a worthy man is ever on his lips He will be pained when he cannot speak all that is in it iii iv  It is sometimes difficult for really good people to forbear doing something more than goodness requires of them iii iv  A Good Man in humble imitation of the Almighty will be an encourager of the penitent and an humbler of the impenitent iii iv  If a Good Man cannot be happy in his own affairs he will rejoice in every opportunity put into his hands to promote the felicity of others iii iv  To be respected by the worthy is to be ranked as one of them iv  This reward have good persons that those who will not imitate them nevertheless revere them iv  Those who love a Good Man do honour to themselves iv  A Good Man will take his measures of right and wrong from his conscience only iv v  Whether his goodness be gracefully accepted or not he will rejoice in having been enabled to do a just and generous thing  ibid  Let a Good Man travel all the world over he will go from friend to friend iv v  A Good Man is superior to all attempts that are not grounded on honour and conscience iv v  Women of slight fame have no way to come at a prudent and virtuous man  ibid  While women of virtue are secure from any attempts of his  ibid  The Good Man will be ready to question the rectitude of his own heart if on examination he has not reason to hope that charity is the principal of his graces iv v  It is a great point gained with a Good Man when on looking impartially back on his own conduct on some great event whether prosperous or not that he is entirely satisfied with himself iv v  A generous Man can enjoy the reward in the good action and look for no other iv v  Such a one can forget his own interest when a right and just measure is to be taken iv v  The Man who in the greater actions of his life thinks himself under the Allseeing eye will not be afraid of a fellowcreature's ear iv v  An impartial spirit will admire goodness or greatness whereever he meets with it and whether it makes for or against him iv v  The conduct of a good and prudent man will be apt to make a considerate person who has connexions with him afraid since if there be a fault between them it will probably be all that person's iv v  A Good Man will be able to pray that the Almighty will in mercy withhold from him wealth or affluence and make him dependent even for his daily bread were riches to be a snare to him and were he not to find his inclinations to do good as occasions offered enlarge with his power iv v  Intrepidity and tenderness are inseparable qualities in the heart of a man truly brave and good v  A Good Man can be happy in his own company v vi  Low narrow jealousies will never enter the heart of a Good Man v vi  From whom can spirit chearfulness debonnairness be expected if not from a Good Man v vi  Austerity uncharitableness on one hand ostentation affectation on the other are qualities which can have no place in the heart of a Good Man v vi  What a blessing to all around him is a Good Man v vi  A Good Man will have an unbounded charity and universal benevolence to men of all professions Imitateing the Divinity he will regard the heart rather than the head and much more than rank or fortune even were it princely Yet is no leveller but on the contrary thinks that rank or degree intitles a man not utterly unworthy of his rank to respect v vi  A Good Man will be able to pity and console a dying friend without saddening his own heart for living the life of duty as he goes along he fears not the inevitable lot vi vii  Good Men by perseverance and uniformity may bring all their friends and visitors to allow of and occasionally to join in his family devotion vi vii  A Man of Honour is more valuable to a single woman in trouble than all the riches of the east vi vii  A Good Man has an interest in every worthy person's affections vi vii  The intention of a Man of Honour is his act vi vii  A Good Man will rather be a sufferer than an aggressor vi vii  A Good Man cannot allow himself to palliate or temporize with a duty vi vii  The intervention and character of a Good man will obviate many difficulties  ibid  What cannot he effect What force has his example  ibid  His love his friendships are to be gloried in Magnanimity and tenderness are united in his noble heart  ibid  Littleness of any kind has no place in it  ibid  All who know him are studious to commend themselves to his favourable opinion they will be sollicitous about what he will think of them and suppressing common foibles before him find their hearts expand and will not know how to be mean  ibid  What is there in the boasted character of most of those who are called HEROES to the unoftentatious merit of a GOOD Man In what a variety of amiable lights does such a one appear In how many ways is he a  blessing and joy to his fellowcreatures vi vii  See  Beneficence Charity  in Judgment  Duties  Moral and Religious  Example Friendship Generosity Goodness Wife should be acquainted with the theory of the domestic duties and not be ashamed occasionally to enter into the direction of the practic i  ibid    A Good Woman reflects honour on all those who had any hand in her education and on the company she has kept i  ibid    Such a one will not allow herself to marry any man with the hopes of his death ii  A Good Woman is one of the greatest glories of the creation ii ii iii  A woman of virtue of good understanding of family skilled in and delighting to perform the duties of the domestic life needs not fortune to recommend her to the choice of the greatest and richest man who wishes his own happiness iii iv  A Good Woman's prospects of happiness with a good man reach into eternity v vi  See  Advice  to Women  Education Female  Dignity  Marriages Modesty Prudence  Single  Women  Gratitude Ingratitude  WHO that lies under the weight of an unreturnable obligation can view the obliger but with the most delicate sensibilities i  ibid    There are dark spirits who are capable of hating the persons who oblige them beyond return i  ibid    Where high respect is entertained grateful hearts will be always ready to accuse themselves of imperfections  which no one else can charge them with i ii  Grateful hearts will always retain a sense of favours conferred upon them ii  Too great obligations from one side will create awe and distance from the other  ibid  A grateful mind will be thankful for benefits past altho' its further expectations should not be answered ii  Love and Gratitude cannot be easily separated iii iv  If a benefactor comply not with all our hopes we ought to retain a grateful sense of the benefits we have actually received at his hands iii iv  A right heart cannot be ingrateful iii iv  Little offices if done with tolerable grace will make a noble spirit think itself under obligation vi vii  See  Generosity  Grief Melancholy Tears  WHEN the heart is softened either by Grief or Pity light impressions will go deep i  ibid    Surprizes from joy are generally sooner recovered from than those from Grief i  ibid    Tears when time has matured a pungent Grief into a sweet melancholy are not hurtful They are as the dew of the morning to the green herbage ii  There is a pleasure as well as pain in Melancholy iii  A talkative Grief is sooner got over than a silent one iv v  When the  whole  mind is taken up with a disastrous subject the entering into it with a faithful friend cannot be a renewal of Grief iv v  Tho' we need not seek for melancholy subjects to give us distress of mind yet we ought not perhaps to shun them when they naturally come to our knowlege v vi  A shy young woman  observes Lady G  may possibly  when a subject of Grief occurs be caught by her lover in a weeping fit v vi  The heart softened by Grief will turn to a comforter  ibid  A woman's own cause of Grief produces pity for another Pity begets love They are next neighbours and will call in to ask kindly how the sufferer does  ibid  And what a heart must  Grief  have if it will not be grateful to  love  when it makes its neighbourly call  ibid  A truly beneficent man will find out the sighing heart and relieve it before it is overwhelmed with calamity vi vii  See  Adversity Consolation  Guardian Guardianship Ward  WHERE the reputation of a lady is concerned a Guardian not in years should not depend too much upon his own character however unexceptionable ii iii  A worthy Guardian will endeavour to make his female ward the wife of a man's love rather than of his own convenience iii iv  A generous Guardian will not require implicit obedience from his ward iii iv  A good man will be very careful in the case of his Guardianship how he advances himself or family by virtue of the trust reposed in him v vi  Were his ward to be advanced by an alliance with him he not with her or were the advantage reciprocal in those cases is the Guardian justified  ibid  But in this delicate case the Guardian putting himself in the situation of the deceased parent and acting as he conscientiously thinks the parent if living would have done he will have the best rule for his conduct on such an occasion  A young woman from fourteen to twenty is often a troublesome charge on a friendly heart v vi  A Guardian will be very careful not to influence his ward in favour of his friend or relation however near  and dear he is to him especially if she has a great fortune vi vii  But if such a near relation or friend and the young lady voluntarily prefer each other to every one else and the young lady mean not a compliment to him he will not upon mere motives of delicacy stand in their way  ibid    See  Indulgence Parents  and Children  H  Happiness Unhappiness  HE that builds his Happiness on the favour of the great pins his tranquillity to the feather in another man's cap ii iii  In the general oeconomy of providence none of the sons of men are unhappy but some others are the happier for it iii  High pleasure and high pain are very near neighbours They are often guilty of excesses and then are apt to mistake each other's house iii  That only is Happiness which we think so iii iv  An easy heart goes a great way to the cure of desperate maladies v vi  In the domestic or private life after all the bustle and parade that can be made lies the true because untumultuous Happiness vi vii  When we are not quite happy in our own thoughts it is a relief to carry them out of ourselves vi vii  The Happiness of human life is at best but comparative vi vii  The utmost Happiness we should hope for in this life is such a situation as with a selfapproving mind will carry us best thro' this scene of trial  ibid  That woman may be said to be happy who marries the man of her choice and he chosen by her friends and generally esteemed by those who know him  ibid  See  Duties  Moral and Religious  Good Man Marriage Prudence  HONEST MAN  See  Good Man  Honour Glory Punctilio Reputation  THE word or Ingenuousness Virtue  Human  Nature  HUMAN Nature is pretty much the same in every country allowing for different customs and different educati n i  ibid    If the human mind is not actively good it will generally be actively evil i ii  A clergyman who is an honour to his cloth may be said to be an ornament to Human Nature i ii  Libertinism by some is called a knowlege of the world a knowlege of Human Nature but the character of Human Nature it is hoped is not to be taken from the overflowings of dirty imaginations ii  Attention love admiration cannot be always kept upon the stretch Human Nature will not bear it iii iv  On the return of a longabsent friend for example the rapture lasts not more than an hour  ibid  Gladdened as the heart is the friend received and the friend receiving perhaps in less than an hour can sit down quietly together to hear and tell stories of what has happened to either in their longregretted absence  ibid  Human Nature in general is not so bad a thing as some disgracers of their own species have seemed to imagine iv v  Humility  HUMILITY and good humour will give a weak man a preference to an arrogant or conceited one i  ibid    To look forward to those who excel us rather than backward to those whom we suppose we excel is necessary to obtain the grace of Humility i  ibid    The Humility and Diffidence of a worthy young person will be increased with the trust and confidence reposed in his or her discretion ii  See  Modesty  HUMOUR  See  Ridicule  Husband  and Wife  A WOMAN is more the property of her husband than he is hers i  ibid    Those personal qualities which make a man generally admired sometimes occasion an abatement in his Wife's happiness i  ibid    It is a transporting thing for an affectionate Wife to receive a worthy Husband returning to her after a long absence or an escaped danger i ii  The man of middle capacity  Lady G says  makes the best Husband to a woman who has talents i ii  Such a one knows just enough  she says  to induce him to admire in her what he has not in himself  ibid  If she has prudence enough to give him consequence before folks she will be able to manage him as she pleases  ibid  But a fool and a wit are equally unmanageable i ii  Managing women are not generally the best to live with i ii  Married women need not to look out of their families so often as some of them do for employment and that not only of the most useful but of the most delightful sort i ii  The woman who has a gay Husband should never refuse  him her company abroad when he desires it ii  The prudent woman who has an expensive Husband will endeavour if she cannot restrain him by her oeconomy to enable him to support his extravagancies with as little discredit to himself or hurt to his family as possible ii  The duty of a worthy Wife will be founded in principle not in tameness or servility ii  The vices of a Husband call forth the virtues of a Wife ii  A Wife can do no more than her duty by a Husband who is not a savage ii iv ii v  The most happily married woman must have a will to which she must resign her own or break her marriage vow ii iii  A Wife may allow in general of a Husband's superior understanding where it is very apparent but in particular cases and as they fall out one by one the man may find it difficult to have it allowed in any one instance ii iii  There should not be a rivalry in particular qualities between Man and Wife ii iii  The world will find occasions enow for exercising the patience of a married pair without their needing to study for them ii iv iii iv  Contempt or the appearance of it in a Wife what man can bear iii  The exasperated spirit of a meek man is more to be apprehended than the sudden gusts of anger of a passionate one iii iii iv  A lively woman who marries a man of inferior understanding ought to be more careful of restraining her vivacity than she need to be if the difference were in his favour iii  The woman who depreciates her husband still more depreciates herself  ibid  The woman who sets out regardless of her Husband's displeasure may make her petulance habitual to him and live to rejoice in seeing him pleased with her iii iv  Men in the former age used to have many ways that women had not to divert themselves abroad when they could not be happy at home iii iv  But modern women as  Lady G observes  can every hour of the twentyfour be up with their monarchs  if they are undutiful  iii iv  If a woman would have the world respect her Husband she must set the example iii iv  The Wife who gives the least room to suspect that she despises her Husband subjects him to contempt if he resent it not and if he do can she be happy  ibid  There is a kind of immorality in the public fondness of a married pair iv  A woman cannot more effectually dishonour herself than by exposing her Husband iv  A fond Husband  Lady G says  is a surfeiting thing yet she believes most women love to be made monkeys of iv  Lord and master  says Lady G  do not always go together tho' they do too often for the happiness of many a meek soul of our sex iv  Those Husbands  says the same lively Lady  are not to be forgiven who will argue when they have nothing to say iv  Many Husbands praise their Wives many Wives their Husbands  Lady G says  to do credit to their choice who were they at their option would be hanged rather than renew their bargain iv v  Happy the Husband happy the wife who on the death of either has no material cause of selfreproach on reflecting on his or her behaviour to the departed iv v  When harmony reigns between a wedded pair their very foibles will make them shine in every eye v  If a Husband has foibles a wife should be very careful how she exposes him for them v  The tender and polite yet discreet behaviour of a Husband to his Wife in public does as much credit to his own heart as to her v vi  Real and unaffected tenderness from a healthy Wife to  an Husband labouring under sickness or the infirmities of age is the very essence of generous love vi vii  A good Husband and good Wife are the world to each other vi vii  An Husband seldom cares to be convinced by a Wife's arguments the less if he is jealous of the superiority of her understanding vi vii  What hopes then can a woman have of reforming an habitual rake who holds her cheap perhaps for the very choice she has made of him in preference to a better man  A scoffer is a wit in his own opinion his conceit as well as profligacy will render him impenetrable to a Wife's arguments tho' ever so reasonable and conclusive vi vii  It seems necessary to the happiness of common minds in wedlock that the woman should have a greater opinion of her Husband's understanding than she has of her own vi vii  See  Good  Wife  Love reliance placed by parents and guardians in the discretion of a young lady who wants not gratitude will make her more difficult in the disposing of herself than if she were made uneasy by distrust and confinement i  ibid    How can palsied age  says the good old Mrs Shirley  which affords but a terrifying object to youth except the Indulgence the love of the young and gay if it do not study to promote those pleasures of which itself was fond in youth v vi  Enjoy innocently your season girls  once said she  setting half a score of young ladies into countrydances  ibid  I watch for the failure of my memory and shall never give it over for quite lost till I have forgotten what were my own innocent wishes and delights in the days of my youth  ibid  The way to judge of the propriety of a present Indulgence is to look back to what we should have thought of it before we allowed ourselves in it vi vii  By an Indulgence growing upon us in the passed year we may be apprehensive of the head it will gain upon us in the next if not prudently restrained  ibid  See  Generosity Good Man Gratitude Guardian Parents  and Children  Inferiority  Superiority of the two Sexes  MEN in the pride of their hearts are apt to suppose that nature has designed them to be superior to women the highest proof that can be given of such superiority is the protection afforded by the stronger to the weaker iii  What can that man say for himself or for his haughty pretension who employs all his arts to seduce betray and ruin the creature whom he should guide and protect  ibid  After all  says Miss Byron  speaking of courage in men I think we must allow a natural superiority in the minds of men over women Do we not want protection And does not that want imply Inferiority v vi  Yet if there be two sorts of courage  acquired  and  natural  why may not the former be obtained by women as well as by men were they to have the same education  Natural  courage may belong to either  ibid  But women have more silly antipathies than men which help to keep them down Which however may be owing rather to affectation at first than to natural imbecility of mind  ibid  A frog a toad a spider a beetle an earwig will give us  proceeds she  mighty pretty tender terror while the heroic men will trample under foot the insect and look the braver for their barbarity and for our delicate screaming  ibid  But for an adventure  concludes she  if a lover gets us into one we frequently leave him a great way behind us  ibid  For the notion of the Inferiority and Superiority of men and women see the amicable debate between Lady G Mrs Shirley Sir Charles Grandison and others  Vol v Let lviii Vol vi Let lv  Ingenuousness Impartiality Justice  AN ingenuous mind will not be afraid of a monitor i  ibid    We ought to judge of our friends as they deserve not as being our friends i  ibid    A noble mind will be ever ready on conviction to acknowlege its mistakes i  ibid    Esteem and love should be founded on merit not on mere relation ii  Mercy and justice are sistergraces and in a virtuous bosom should not be separated ii  No one can judge properly of another that cannot in imagination be that other when he takes the judgmentseat ii  iv ii v  The laws of truth and justice are ever the same ii  What others would  not  have done in a like situation will not be considered by a good man ii  Justice will be thought a severe thing by the unjust ii  A man of intrinsic merit will not seek to raise his own character at the expence of that of another ii  Tho' I say it that should not say it  a faulty phrase when it is spoken of a deserving relation ii  Mercy should never be separated from justice ii  It is a weakness to look without abatement of esteem on those faults in one person which we should hold utterly inexcusable in another ii iii  A worthy man will not plead his privilege to defend himself against a legal pursuer ii iii  The person who treats even a faulty person with injustice or hardship makes himself enemies and the criminal friends pitiers at least  ibid  Let not even the faulty have just cause to complain of us ii iii  We should do proper things for our own sakes whether persons are capable of gratitude or not ii iii  In cases of right and wrong we ought not to know either friend or relation iii  For the honour of the sex let it not be said that a woman whose glory is compassionate tenderness is not to be prevailed upon to do an act of kindness much less of justice iii iv  An ingenuous person who has insisted upon a wrong measure will when convinced recede with a grace iii iv  We ought not to be displeased with nor depreciate the person who cannot do for us or be to us all we wish iii iv  It is a degree of merit to acknowlege with some grace an error iv  The acknowlegement of a fault affords as much honour as could be gained by a victory iv v  Were the aggressor in a quarrel the nearest and dearest of all others to us an impartial person will condemn him and espouse the cause of the sufferer iv v  To what we know to be right we ought to submit the more difficult it is to do so the more praiseworthy v vi  To do justice against ourselves is intitling ourselves at least to a second merit  ibid  A person is guilty of false heroism who in doing more justice than is due to one person does less than is due to another vi vii  See  Good Man Modesty See  Ingenuousness  Justices  of Peace  WOULD persons of sense and distinction more frequently than they do act as Justices of Peace the office would be lighter to every one and would keep the great power vested in this class of magistrates and which is every year increasing out of mean and mercenary hands vi vii  And do not men of consideration in the world owe it to their tenants and neighbours to employ in their service those advantages of rank and education which make it easy for them to clear up and adjust matters that would be of endless perplexity to the parties concerned  ibid  K mistress which they would not concede to a worthy wife ii  Keepers made by their vices real slaves imagine themselves masters of their liberty and sit down satisfied with the sound of the word  ibid  The reputation of a woman is above all price ii iii  Every quarterly payment every present made to a person engaged in a guilty Attachment must strike her to the heart when overtaken by compunction to behold in it the wages of her shame ii iii  The woman who has forfeited her own reputation will not be careful of the man's  ibid  Folly encounters with folly in a Guilty Attachment ii iii  Unprincipled women bear testimony to the honour of  virtue by the high price they generally set upon their first departure from it ii iii  The woman who stipulates a price for her virtue knows the uncertainty of the tenure by which she holds  ibid  How can a Keeper complain of the misbehaviour of his woman be it ever so base Does she not first misbehave to herself to her sex and break thro' all laws divine and human ii iii  Ought a man who brings a woman to violate her first duties to expect from her a regard to a mere discretionary obligation  ibid  It must be a wretchedness beyond what can be conceived for a man and woman to live together a life of guilt yet with hatred animosity or even indifference to each other  ibid  God knows what he will forgive but his forgiveness however depends in a great measure upon the offenders themselves ii iii  Where hatred or dislike has once taken place of likeing in a Guilty Attachment the first separation is always best  ibid  Guilty persons render themselves contemptible in the eyes even of those very minions who administer to their unlawful pleasures  ibid  The woman who has not virtue has no title to spirit or resentment  ibid  The daughter of a cottager who keeps her virtue is superior to the greatest man on earth who seeks to corrupt her He himself allows her to be so in the court he makes to her as well as by the indignity he offers both to her and himself ii iii  Keepers are generally tho' bravoes of the law cowards and cullies to their paramours ii iii  Indeed the courage of the men who can defy the laws of society to magnanimity they must be strangers is ever to be doubted  ibid  The Keeper by the same act sinks his own consequence and generally raises that of an inferior and lowbred woman ii iii  The private man who quarrels with his woman for no reason but to take another is a worse man than Henry VIII for he allowed not himself to be either a Keeper or Polygamist ii iii  Guilty Attachments are often the cause of mens despising a legal one iii  And what are the invectives of free livers against the legal one but meanly studied attempts to justify the way of life they have fallen into  ibid  A good heart a delicate mind cannot associate with a corrupt one  ibid  What tie can bind a woman who has parted with her honour  ibid  What in a Guilty Attachment must be a man's alternative but either to be the tyrant of a wretch who has given him reason to despise her or the dupe of one who despises him  ibid  It is the important lesson of life in the present union of soul and body to restrain the unruly appetites of the latter and to improve the faculties of the former  ibid  Can this end be restrained by licentious indulgencies and profligate associations  ibid  How much in the power of women  Learning Learners Languages Science University  THE man of the town and the pedant are two extreme points in comparison with each other i  ibid    Vast is the field of Science The more a man knows the more he will find he has to know i  ibid    Goodnature a general philanthropy but not a love of persons for their faults mark the true satirist i  ibid    The world is one great University i  ibid    The knowlege that is to be obtained in the lesser University should not make a man despise what is to be acquired in the greater in which that knowlege was principally intended to make him useful i  ibid    Smatterers in Learning are the most opinionated i  ibid    Learning consists not in the knowlege of Languages i  ibid    The first great genius had not human example and human precepts to improve by i  ibid    Language is but a vehicle to Science it is not Science itself i  ibid    Great respect should however be paid to Linguists but ought that confusion of tongues which was intended use to the greater purposes of life and knowlege  ibid  People who least know how to argue are generally the most contentious  ibid  Homer Virgil Milton were learned men yet wrote their immortal works p    to    See  Education Learned  Women  Learned  Women  A LEARNED Woman with her own sex is as an owl among the lesser birds i  ibid    Men generally are afraid of a wife who has more understanding than themselves  ibid  Is it a necessary consequence that that knowlege which shall make a man shine must make a woman vain and pragmatical  ibid  May not a lettered wife and a learned husband be flint and steel to each other  ibid  Many men like the Turks think that empire safest which is founded in ignorance  ibid  In what a situation are women who if they have genius will be thought guilty of affectation whether they appear desirous to conceal it or submit to have it called forth i  ibid    Women should not be ashamed either of their talents or acquirements i  ibid    They should only take care not to give up their domestic usefulness for Learning  ibid  They will then by reason of their acquirements be more suitable companions for men of sense and learning  ibid  The man must have a narrow mind who is apprehensive of his prerogative from a Learned Woman  ibid  A woman who does not behave the better the more she knows would make her husband uneasy and think as well of herself were she totally illiterate  ibid  Do not men court for wives whom they please A conceited a vain mind in a woman cannot be hidden  ibid  Young women who are writers should not suffer their pen to run away with their needle i  ibid    Nor their love of reading to interfere with that housewifery which is an indispensible in the character of a good woman  ibid  Tho' Learning should not be the principal distinction of a woman yet where talents are given they should not be either uncultivated or unacknowleged  ibid  When no duty is neglected for the acquirement when modesty elegance and a teachable spirit are preserved it is not a disgrace to a woman to be supposed to know something i  ibid    Why should women in compliance with the petulance of narrowminded men forbear to use a word which indicates knowlege when no other single word will so well express their sense i  ibid    Yet the confining themselves to the usuallyknown and familiar words in language will always give them a simplicity and ease that will make them shine in the true grace of expression above men especially above the generality of scholastic men  A young woman will rather choose to distinguish herself by her discretion and prudence than by her wit and poetry ii  Yet the easy productions of a fine fancy not made the business of life or its boast confer no denomination that is disgraceful but very much the contrary  ibid  Libertines Rakes  LIBERTINE men consider all intellectual attainments in women as either useless or impertinent i  ibid    A wise a learned woman they look upon as an unnatural character  ibid  They want women to be all love and nothing else  ibid  A man of free principles shewn by practices as free can hardly make a tender husband i  ibid    Who shall trust for the performance of his  second  duties the man who avowedly despises his  first ibid  The profligate who had a good education must have  taken pains to render vain the precepts of his instructors and still more to make a jest of them i  ibid    The man must have a very hard heart as well as be a most abandoned man who can pass from woman to woman without remorse for a former whom he has by solemn vows seduced i  ibid    Of what a dreadful abuse of passions given for the noblest purposes are profligate men guilty  ibid  Libertines have all one dialect They say nothing new or deserving attention i i ii  Libertines delight to sport with the healths and happiness of credulous young creatures whom they pretend to love i  ibid    A man who has insulted a woman must have a high opinion of himself and a low one of her if he thinks marriage will be an atonement or do her honour i  ibid    Rakish men are generally suspicious tyrants They live in continual fear of retribution ii  Men of strong health and of a riotous turn should not in mere compassion associate with men of weaker constitutions than their own ii  Neither should extravagant men of high and low fortunes associate since the expences which will but shake the estate of the one will demolish that of the other ii  Rakish men seldom make good husbands good fathers good brothers ii  Libertines are generally narrowhearted creatures who center all their delights in themselves ii  The notion that a reformed Rake makes the best husband is equally vulgar and pernicious and becomes only the mouth of an inconsiderate woman ii  Rakish men frequently endeavour to justify their vices by general reflexions on the sex which so often prefers them to better men ii  Such men think one half of a woman's virtue is pride the other cunning which they call the wisdom of women  ibid  Were they sure  says Sir Thomas Grandison  that the man would not think the worse of them for their forwardness they would not wait for a second question ii  Who shall answer for the durable reformation of an habitual Libertine ii  Mercy is a virtue Can it be expected from the wicked ii  A seducing Rake has more souls to answer for than his own ii  It is an high degree of confidence in a man of free principles to think of approaching a woman of piety and prudence with any hope of success ii  Rakes often have contentions with themselves which they own to one another whether they shall  vouchsafe  to offer themselves in marriage to a lady before they conclude to do her that favour ii  Those who pretend to know the sex best think themselves intitled among one another to treat it with the least respect ii  The most profligate men love modesty in the sex at the very time they are forming plots to destroy it in individuals of it Sight Clandestine  Marriages Modesty Protestations  Single  Women Seduction  Love  LOVE dignifies the adored object in the eye of a lover i  ibid    True Love is always modest and diffident i  ibid    Trifles are acceptable from those we love i  ibid    Women ought not to be ashamed of owning a susceptibility of a natural passion when duty and prudence are their guides and the object worthy i  ibid    Women when they begin to  like  should look into their hearts since  Love  is not then far off  ibid  Young women often given way to a passion which they  suppose  unconquerable because they will not take pains to subdue it  ibid  A prudent woman will shut the door of her heart against the blind deity when she finds he has set his incroaching foot on the threshold i  ibid    Such a one will endeavour to keep her heart her own till duty adds force to the lambent flame i  ibid    Making Love  as it is called is an  ungenerous  abuse of the ears of a young woman since a man can address whom he pleases and a woman must wait his motions and since we are all ready to believe what we wish i  ibid    A man who truly loves cannot without pain allow himself to teaze by importunity the object of his passion who favours him not i  ibid    One of the greatest pains that a grateful heart can know is to be obliged to deny a worthy man who tenderly loves her i  ibid    Respectful Love is not the indication either of a weak head or a faint heart i  ibid    Violent Love is not likely to be lasting i  ibid    It is one of the sweetest pleasures imaginable to hear a whole circle join in applauding the absent person who stands high in our opinion i ii  Love is a selfmortifier i ii  Love is made a cover to baseness of heart when the pretender to it seeks to gratify his own passion at the expence of the happiness or duty of the object ii  In a pure flame Love and Friendship cannot be separated  ibid  Love is a word that is often made a cover to the vilest cupidity ii  Love however dignified by romancers and poets is a narrower of the heart since while its success is in suspence it is the parent of envy jealousy dissimulation ii iii ii iii  That Love which is founded on fancy or exterior advantages may and oftentimes ought to be overcome ii  But that which is founded on interior worth generally acknowledged cannot be easily restrained damped suppressed ii v ii vi  Women do not often with  earnestness  reject a man who is not quite disagreeable if they are not prejudiced in another's favour ii  It is no wonder that a man who is always worthily employ'd is not in love since Love is the child of leisure and indolence ii iii ii iv  A woman who means not preferable favour to a man will allow herself in saying civil and polite things to him ii  There are subjects that cannot be touched upon without raising emotion in the bosom of a person who hopes and is uncertain ii  Why should women be ashamed of owning a  laudable  passion There is nothing shameful in discreet Love ii iv ii iv  Love is a selfish deity He puts two persons upon preferring their own interest nay often a gratification of their passion against their interest to those of everybody else and reason discretion duty are frequently given up in a competition with it ii  But Love nevertheless will not do every thing for the ardent pair  ibid  Parents know this and ought not to suffer for the rashness they wish to prevent but cannot ii  Is a father who has by his prudence weathered many a storm and got safe into port obliged to reimbark in the voyage of life with the young people who perhaps in a little while will look upon him as an incumbrance and grudge him his cabin  ibid  Parents should be indulgent to those who are to succeed them in carrying on the business of the world But children when they put themselves in one scale should allow parents their due weight in the other  ibid  A worthy woman will banish that love from her heart which would corrupt its simplicity and make her guilty of artifice ii iii  Womens eyes are wanderers and too often bring home guests that are very troublesome and whom once introduced they cannot get out of the house ii iii  The voice as well as the heart is sweetened mellowed harmonized by Love ii iii iii iv  It is a hard matter for women in Lovecases to avoid affectation ii iii  A lady in suspending unnecessarily the happiness of the man to whom she has no objection and whom she resolves to marry seems to confess selfdenial ii iii  Esteem is the female word for Love ii iii  The woman who has been disappointed in Love will be able to play with another flame without burning her fingers iii  A prudent man or woman will never leave MIND out of his or her notion of Love iii  Women often take pains to cheat themselves into a belief that they are not in Love but detect themselves by the very pains they take to obviate such a charge iii  Of what absurd things does the passion called Love makes its votaries guilty iii  The woman who circumscribes her passion by the laws  of reason and duty will never subject herself to lasting disturbance from her recollections iii  The tenderness and even veneration which a worthy man shews to a deservedlybeloved object is not a derogation from his character iii  Love like water will work its way thro' the banks that are set up to confine it if not watched and dammed out in time iii  The poison of Love once taken in at the eye will soon diffuse itself thro' the mass iii  Seldom as the women suppose is there that fervor in a second love as in a first iii  Women in Lovecases see into each other's hearts from small openings iii iv  A young woman challenged with loving a man who has made no declaration if the challenge be just need not to labour under a greater difficulty  ibid  Reverence will not easily allow of the innocent familiarity that is requisite in love iii iv  What a remembrancer is the heart in a Lovecase Not a circumstance escapes it iii iv  If a woman loves a man for his merit  only  may she not love merits equally great in another and even in one of her own sex iii iv  True Love makes every task easy every burthen light iii iv  Young people often in compliment to their own understandings persevere in a first flame which ought to be discouraged  ibid  Love gilds every object that bears a relation to the object beloved iv  There is  says Lady  G a great deal of freemasonry in Love The secret like that when found out is hardly worth the knowing The Paphian Love she must mean iv  Love however at best is a flame founded on a but supposed merit The proof may be wanting in matrimony iv v  What is that Love which we women  says Lady G  vow at the altar Surely not adoration iv v  Not a preference of that object absolutely as in excellence superior to every imaginable being iv v  No more surely in most cases than such a preferable choice all circumstances considered as shall make a woman with satisfaction of mind and with an affectionate and faithful heart unite herself for life with a man she esteems  ibid  Who she thinks is no disagreeable companion but deserves her grateful regard  ibid  That his interest from thenceforth should be her own and his happiness her study  ibid  And is not this very consistent with seeing and pitying in this partner of her life such imperfections as make him evidently the inferior of angels  ibid  Disappoinment in Love may operate in different minds different ways iv v  Young women often go on courageously with a Lover while the end in view is distant or there have been difficulties to encounter with iv v  But when those difficulties are overcome and they have climbed the hill they toiled up they often look about them with fear as strong as their hope  ibid  The controul of those we truly love is freedom iv v  How sweet is the affiance which a woman has in the declaration of a man of principle whom she loves v vi  Lovematches  Lady G says  are generally foolish things v vi  Violent Love on one side is enough in conscience if the other party be not a fool or ingrateful  ibid  The wallclimbers the hedge and ditchleapers the riverforders the windowdroppers always find reason to think it so  ibid  Who ever  proceeds she  hears of darts flames cupids venus's and such sort of nonsense in matrimony  ibid  Passion is transitory but discretion that never boils over gives durable happiness  ibid  Love merely personal that sort which commences  between the age of fifteen and twenty and where there is no extraordinary merit in the object may and generally ought to be subdued v vi  What tumults what a contrariety of passions break the tranquility of the woman who yields up her heart to Love The more as custom has made the man entire master of the question and the woman can but follow as he thi  fit to lead v vi  A man of sense in Love will preserve his dignity yet for his own sake will give consequence to the lady whom he one day hopes to call his wife v vi  Love will plead for its votary in a single breast when consultation on the supposed fit and unfit the object absent will produce delay v vi  A little cold water will quench a beginning flame in a young person or if it do not its blaze may be directed by prudent management to another and more proper object v vi  Love on one side discretion on the other is much better than love on both since in the latter case if the Love be of the usual giddy sort there can be no room at all for discretion v vi  Love ever makes a woman think meanly of herself in proportion as she thinks highly of the object v vi  It is a circumstance to be wished for in a Loveaffair that the affection of the man should be first engaged vi vii  A man of honour who is not disengaged himself will be very careful of engaging the affections of a young woman vi vii  What a sad gradation is there in that Love which tho' begun in hopelesness of succeeding rises by selfflattery to a possibility then to probability and to hope and sinking again to hopelesness ends in despair vi vii  Reason and duty will give a preference in the affection of a prudent woman to the man who is most suitable to her vi vii  Esteem heightened by gratitude and enforced by duty will soon ripen into Love vi vii  A tender a faithful affection is the only sort of Love that suits this imperfect state  ibid  There is a superior ardor that is due only to supreme perfection vi vii  Love authorized by reasonable prospects and guided and heightened by duty is everything excellent that poets have said of it vi vii  Yet even  this  Love must submit to the awful dispensations of Providence whether of death or other disappointment  ibid  Such trials ought to be met with chearful resignation and not to be the means of embittering our lives or of rendering them useless  ibid  Love is a passion that is oftener the cause of meanness than of laudable greatness vi vii  See  Advice  and  Cautions  to Women  Compliments Daughters Delicacy Example
